Grumman)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/12/sr/p012srk7.jpg","Title":"x-47B-index.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p012srk7","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p012srk7","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p012srk7","_id":"559a5b33157e584f77e190d5"}],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Sharon is a 2012/13 fellow at the Woodrow Wilson\nInternational Center for Scholars in Washington DC, where she is working on a\nhistory of the Pentagon's Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency. Her\nwriting on military science and technology has appeared in Nature, Discover,\nSlate, Wired, the Washington Post Magazine, and the Financial Times, among\nother publications. She is the co-author of A Nuclear Family Vacation: Travels\nin the World of Atomic Weaponry (Bloomsbury, 2008) and the author of Imaginary Weapons:\nA Journey Through the Pentagon's Scientific Underworld (Nations Books, 2006).&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Sharon Weinberger","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"ae67fb1f-feba-497e-9404-7677e5d775e1","Id":"wwfuture/author/sharon-weinberger","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"sharon-weinberger"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/sharon-weinberger","_id":"559a5a54157e584f77e17172"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;As a fighter plane prepares to take off from a naval carrier at sea, the pilot and deck crew go through a tightly choreographed series of hand signals to tell each other they are ready to launch. It ends with a final &ldquo;salute&rdquo; from the pilot to indicate that the aircraft is ready to be catapulted off the deck.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But when the X-47B, the US Navy&rsquo;s newest prototype combat aircraft, prepares for its first carrier launch early next year, there will be no salute.&nbsp; That&rsquo;s because there will also be no pilot. Instead, the X-47B will blink its wingtip navigation lights, a robotic nod to the human salute (and mimicking what the Navy does for night launches), before the catapult officer presses the launch button, and the robotic aircraft is flung off the front of the ship&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;After years of development, and recent land-based tests, the highly anticipated carrier flight for this stealthy, tailless, unmanned drone is imminent. &ldquo;It should be in early in 2013,&rdquo; says Carl Johnson, vice president and program manager at defence firm Northrop Grumman, which builds the X-47B. &ldquo;We have to coordinate ship schedules as well as all the other airspace issues.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The X-47B is a strike fighter-sized prototype drone developed as part of the United States Navy's UCAS-D (Unmanned Combat Air System Demonstration) programme, which aims to develop technologies necessary to field a combat drone on carriers. As a result, it has folding wings and is built for the rigors of sea life, including salt water, deck handling and of course take-off and landing from an aircraft carrier.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although the X-47B is a prototype, the Navy hopes to actually field operational unmanned combat aircraft on carriers by the end of the decade.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The unmanned &ldquo;flying wing&rdquo; aircraft, which takes some of its design cues from Northrop Grumman&rsquo;s B-2 stealth bomber, is supposed to demonstrate reconnaissance and strike capabilities&mdash;it has a full-sized weapons bay, although the prototype will not fly with weapons.&nbsp; And, unlike existing drones, which are usually remotely &ldquo;flown&rdquo; by pilots once in the air, the X-47B is designed to fly autonomously, with just the occasional click of a mouse from an operator to send it instructions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s a big deal, but it&rsquo;s an extension of something that was already happening,&rdquo; says Peter Singer, a senior fellow at the Brooking Institution in Washington, DC, and the author of Wired for War, a book on the military&rsquo;s robotics revolution.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Forward fire&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The craft was revealed in 2008 but is only now undergoing sea tests aboard the USS Harry S. Truman, including moving around on the carrier. Whilst this kind of trial may not sound remarkable, in some ways it&rsquo;s one of the more challenging steps toward proving that the X-47B, which weighs in at 20,000 kg (44,000 lb) and has a 20m (62 ft) wing span, is ready for flight.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Getting around on a crowded flight deck is difficult, says Johnson, because the aircraft must maneuver very close the edge of the carrier, sometimes pivoting so that it appears that half the airplane is hanging off the ship. &ldquo;The precision involved in doing that is very difficult with a pilot following directions from a person on the deck,&rdquo; says Johnson. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s very difficult to do that as well with an unmanned system.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a result, the engineers have built a wireless remote control device that can be used to move the aircraft around the deck.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The X-47B has already been tested on land in conditions meant to mimic operations on a carrier deck, including a catapult launch, but operating on a real carrier crowded with people and equipment presents fresh challenges. &nbsp;For example, the X-47B must be tested for electromagnetic interference, in other words, making sure that the aircraft&rsquo;s electronic systems don&rsquo;t clash with the myriad radar and emitters that are on a ship.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;While we go through a rigorous test program, you really learn a lot when you&rsquo;re at sea and you&rsquo;re validating your system against the true environment of the carrier,&rdquo; says Johnson.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If all goes well with these tests, the Navy will then be ready for its first at-sea flight. This will likely be a short affair, according to Johnson, and will start with a catapult launch and end with the aircraft landing not on the carrier, but on firm ground. Later that year, the X-47B will also perform an &ldquo;arrested landing,&rdquo; meaning it will land back on the aircraft carrier.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another key flight test will take place in 2014, when the X-47 demonstrates that it can perform autonomous aerial refueling. Currently, the craft has a range of around 3,200km (2,000 miles) and can stay aloft for six hours. But for effective operations, the Navy would like it to stay aloft for longer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Head-to-head&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if all those tests go smoothly, that doesn&rsquo;t mean the X-47B will actually be deployed. The stealthy, aircraft is still merely a prototype. The Navy soon plans to launch a new program to develop an operational unmanned combat aircraft, which will involve fielding up to half a dozen armed drones on carriers by the end of the decade as part of what&rsquo;s called the Unmanned Carrier-Launched Airborne Surveillance and Strike program.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Early next year, the Navy will hold a competition to build the new drone. That means Northrop, the incumbent, will have to compete against other companies, including Lockheed Martin, which built the stealthy RQ-170 (famously captured by Iran), and General Atomics, which makes the familiar Reaper and Predator drones, and Boeing, which developed the X-45, a one-time competitor to Northrop&rsquo;s drone.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even as a prototype, however, the X-47B&rsquo;s upcoming launch from an aircraft carrier, the &ldquo;heart of US naval aviation&rdquo;, marks a significant watershed for drones, says Singer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s one of the places where we haven&rsquo;t seen them yet.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ultimately, the X-47B&rsquo;s upcoming flight is not about proving that drones can work&mdash;that&rsquo;s already been done&mdash;but expanding how and where they are used. &ldquo;The Wright Brothers moment already happened,&rdquo; says Singer. &ldquo;Now we&rsquo;re in the equivalent of 1920s and 1930s.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt; page or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"Where science, security and secrets collide. Defence writer Sharon Weinberger goes behind the closed doors of the labs and projects working on the future of national security.","Name":"Code Red","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Code Red","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:26:38.302606Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"bc48772e-9411-4996-bc62-45072bc8e05b","Id":"wwfuture/column/code-red","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:26:38.302606Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/code-red"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:26:38.302606Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"bc48772e-9411-4996-bc62-45072bc8e05b","Id":"wwfuture/column/code-red","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:26:38.302606Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/code-red"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/code-red","_id":"559a5a68157e584f77e1744f"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17490"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","HeadlineLong":"X-47B stealth drone targets new frontiers","HeadlineShort":"Stealth drone hits new frontiers","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The US Navy's cutting-edge robot fighter plane aims to be the first unmanned aerial vehicle to take-off and land at sea.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In the early morning of 16 June, 2012, a top secret spaceplane made a picture perfect landing at the Vandenberg Air Force Base in California. To those unfamiliar with the vehicle, it might have looked roughly similar to the US space shuttle, the manned spacecraft that shuttled astronauts into space for three decades.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But this spaceplane, called the X-37B Orbital Test Vehicle, is very different. While it looks like a plane, is launched on a rocket, has a cargo bay and uses some of the same technology as the shuttle, such as thermal shielding to protect it during reentry, it is smaller and unmanned. It is designed to stay in orbit for months on end and can automatically land back on Earth. Perhaps more crucially, the Boeing-designed plane is operated by the US Air Force and its mission is a closely held secret, prompting a slew of speculation about its true purpose.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the first X-37B was launched in 2010, amateur satellite spotters have carefully followed the robotic spacecraft&rsquo;s orbit, while those unconnected with the program have speculated that the plane could be anything from an anti-satellite weapon to so-called &ldquo;on demand reconnaissance,&rdquo; shorthand for a spy satellite that can be placed over any country in the world. Compounding the mystery was the launch of a second vehicle in 2011, which stayed in orbit for 469 days, long exceeding the Air Force&rsquo;s stated maximum requirement of 270 days for the spaceplane.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now, a third launch is slated for 11 December, according to an Air Force spokesperson, once again ramping up the rumour mill. So, what do we actually know about the plane?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tactical response&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Early reports focused on the X-37B&rsquo;s seeming resemblance, at least in size and weight, to &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_X-20_Dyna-Soar\"&gt;the X-20 Dynasoar&lt;/a&gt; (short for Dynamic Soarer), a 1950s-era hypersonic vehicle that was envisioned for a variety of military missions, including bombing and sabotaging enemy satellites. However, experts familiar with the X-37B programme emphasized that its technology is actually closer to the recently retired space shuttle (a &lt;a href=\"http://pdf.aiaa.org/preview/2011/CDReadymspace11_2426/PV2011_7315.pdf\"&gt;fact reinforced by Boeings&rsquo; proposal for a crewed version of the vehicle&lt;/a&gt; known as the X-37C). The Air Force blandly described the role of the X-37B in &lt;a href=\"http://www.af.mil/information/factsheets/factsheet.asp?id=16639\"&gt;a factsheet given to media&lt;/a&gt; as a \"reliable, reusable, unmanned space test platform&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Air Force also says the mini-shuttle has two objectives: testing &ldquo;reusable spacecraft technologies&rdquo; and conducting &ldquo;experiments which can be returned to, and examined, on Earth&rdquo;. Again, this is similar to the stated aims of the space shuttle. But many forget that earlier craft also had a secret military role. Although ostensibly a civilian program, it conducted a series of missions from 1982-1992 on behalf of the National Reconnaissance Office, carrying a series of classified spy satellites.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Similarly, most outside experts now agree that it&rsquo;s likely the robotic space plane is being used for some sort of secret reconnaissance. &ldquo;I think the guess that makes most sense is quick-response tactical imaging, meaning hours to a couple of days from request to delivery,&rdquo; says Allen Thomson, a former CIA analyst.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thomson says it is also possible that it could have a more mundane but useful task, such as &ldquo;maintaining up-to-date general purpose mapping imagery.&rdquo; However, if that is the case, Thomson says that it could be a waste of money. &ldquo;I think that the commercial satellites could and should do that cheaper and better than X-37B,&rdquo; he says. It is a view &lt;a href=\"http://www.ucsusa.org/nuclear_weapons_and_global_security/space_weapons/technical_issues/the-x-37b-space-plane.html\"&gt;backed by parts of the scientific community&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, the X-37B launches comes in the middle of a larger debate about the role of government-operated spy satellites, which have proven enormously costly but can provide some of the most advanced imagery, versus commercial satellite imagery. The US intelligence community recently slashed its budget for commercial imagery, indicating that it was going back to greater reliance on its own classified satellites.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The view that the X-37B is a reconnaissance platform is strengthened by observations from amateur satellite watchers, who track the vehicles&rsquo; orbits, and noticed that it has similar orbits to spy satellites and scientific remote sensing craft. In addition, they noticed the craft changing its orbital path several times during its test flights.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is to be expected, says Joan Johnson-Freese, professor of national security studies at the US Naval War College in Newport. &ldquo;The upcoming launch will continue just to see what the vehicle can do,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;One of the things they are testing is maneuverability. The problem with satellites in orbit is they are very predictably in certain orbits at certain times, and thus vulnerable to anti-satellite weapons (ASAT).&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Johnson-Freese says the military has long been interested in the ability of a spacecraft that has &ldquo;the ability to evade, to maneuver, to not be in a predicable place at a predicable time.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;She expects the Air Force will be &ldquo;pressing the envelope for manoeuvrability and duration&rdquo; during the X-37B&rsquo;s next flight. &ldquo;That will give them the idea of potential missions where avoidance of ASATs comes into play.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;No weapons&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although its orbits may be difficult to predict in advance, its tracks show where the X-37B has been and its likely purpose, says Brian Weeden, a technical advisor at the Secure World Foundation, a Washington-based foundation that focuses on space issues.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, he says, the X-37B flew at inclination of 42.79 degrees, which tells you how far north and south in latitude the spacecraft can see. &ldquo;The tradeoff is that something at a 90 degree polar orbit covers the whole world, but its frequency is less; it may arrive only every couple of days,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;If something is 40 or 45 degrees, it would be covering a smaller portion [of the earth], but more often. &ldquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At a 42.79 degree inclination, the X-37B would be useful for looking at a geographic region such as the Middle East, says Weeden, pouring cold water on one theory that that it was used to spy on &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-16423881\"&gt;China&rsquo;s spacelab, Tiangong-1&lt;/a&gt;. And given the current political context, he says, the Middle East &ldquo;makes sense&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weeden also suggests the X-37B&rsquo;s orbit may indicate that the military is trying out a new sensor system, such as radar imaging or &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperspectral_imaging\"&gt;hyperspectral sensors&lt;/a&gt;, which collect information across different wavelengths. He suggests this could be the case, because unlike satellites collecting light in the visible wavelength, the X-37B&rsquo;s orbits are not synchronized with the sun, a trick used to maintain a predictable angle between the sun, satellite and ground.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But, like with many of the theories surrounding the X-37B, he warns, &ldquo;it is just speculation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For its part, the Air Force itself is silent about the plane&rsquo;s use, only speaking to allay fears that it was a weapon. \"I don't know how this could be called weaponisation of space. It's just an updated version of the space shuttle type of activities in space,\" said Gary Payton, the Air Force's deputy undersecretary for space programmes, in 2010.&nbsp; \"We, the Air Force, have a suite of military missions in space and this new vehicle could potentially help us do those missions better.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Whatever its purpose; the X-37B is perhaps one of the few bright spots among the Pentagon&rsquo;s hypersonic test vehicle programs.&nbsp; The Air Force&rsquo;s X-51A Waverider, a scramjet powered hypersonic missile, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120815-hypersonic-ambitions-fall-short\"&gt;suffered a fatal mishap earlier this year on a test flight&lt;/a&gt;, and never reached hypersonic speeds.&nbsp; Separately, another hypersonic prototype, known as the Falcon Hypersonic Test Vehicle-2, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-us-canada-14497641\"&gt;suffered mishaps in both flight tests&lt;/a&gt;, plunging into the ocean.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Those other efforts, which are focused on creating missiles, are still in early testing phase, while the X-37B is clearly further along.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I think the one interesting question is whether this is just test and evaluation, or is it being used to be support real world operations,&rdquo; says Weeden. But like most questions about the plane, it is one that is currently impossible to answer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","HeadlineLong":"X-37B: Secrets of the US military spaceplane","HeadlineShort":"Secrets of US military spaceplane","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"As the US Air Force prepares for the third launch of its mysterious X-37B vehicle, BBC Future looks at what is known about the classified project. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"As the US Air Force prepares for the third launch of its mysterious X-37B vehicle, we look at what is known about the classified project. ","SummaryShort":"What is the mysterious X-37B doing?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"436db3b4-354d-4d3e-9f9a-f618199c2425","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121123-secrets-of-us-military-spaceplane","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121123-secrets-of-us-military-spaceplane"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121123-secrets-of-us-military-spaceplane","_id":"559a5df2157e584f77e202f9"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If you&rsquo;re looking for a headline to capture our technological times, this recent one might fit the bill: &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://spectrum.ieee.org/geek-life/hands-on/the-diy-kidtracking-drone\"&gt;The DIY kid-tracking drone&lt;/a&gt;.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To help keep a close eye on his child, American parent Paul Wallich adapted a quadcopter to follow a GPS chip in his son&rsquo;s backpack to the bus stop every morning on the way to school: an ingenious outsourcing of parental responsibility, and a formidable piece of hacking to boot. What, though, does this say about the increasing delegation not only of daily tasks to machines, but of potentially life-changing decisions themselves?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Take a life and death dilemma that could soon be science fact rather than fiction. As psychology professor Gary Marcus pointed out in a recent &lt;a href=\"http://www.newyorker.com/online/blogs/newsdesk/2012/11/google-driverless-car-morality.html\"&gt;piece for &lt;em&gt;The New Yorker&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, driverless cars are now street-legal in three American states and could soon be cruising into a garage near you. But if your driverless car were hypothetically to encounter a bus full of schoolchildren on the wrong side of the road, how should it react: should it make a decision to swerve, preventing a collision but putting your life at risk; or should it collide with the bus if there&rsquo;s a greater chance of you surviving? &ldquo;If the decision must be made in milliseconds,&rdquo; Marcus reasons, &ldquo;the computer will have to make the call.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Cars are an interesting case study because they already represent one of the most hazardous technological environments most of us enter on a regular basis &ndash; and because the belief that technology should make this environment as safe as possible has been accepted, and been saving lives, for decades. As a famous adage of product design puts it, once you&rsquo;ve built the car, your task is not simply to hope that you can prevent all accidents, but to design a better car crash &ndash; that is, to make those inevitable occasions on which things go wrong as unlikely as possible to cause fatal harm.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Minimising harm is a clear enough good. What happens, though, to ethical issues in design when the product you&rsquo;re creating is itself going to be making decisions?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Killing machine&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Marcus&rsquo; driverless car scenario riffs off the famous &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trolley_problem\"&gt;trolley problem&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo;: a thought experiment that asks subjects to decide between pushing one man off a bridge in order to save the lives of several others trapped in the path of a runaway railway trolley, or allowing that one man to survive while the others meet their doom. Most respondents are unwilling physically to push another human being to their death even if it would save multiple lives &ndash; one of the factors the test is designed to measure. A machine&rsquo;s response, though, would depend entirely upon the decision-making model encoded by its creators. One robot might refuse to act; one might save the many; one might not even recognize a choice exists. It&rsquo;s up to us to decide which program to write. But once we&rsquo;ve done it and set things in motion, it&rsquo;s up to them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Programming this into cars is one thing. Weapons are quite another &ndash; and represent perhaps the most urgent testing-ground for machine ethics today. Early this December, Iran boasted that it had &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-middle-east-20594431\"&gt;captured a US ScanEagle drone&lt;/a&gt; over its airspace; one of the most basic models of the thousands of unmanned aerial vehicles that have become the standard stuff of warfare over the last decade.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Like Paul Wallich&rsquo;s child-minding quadcopter, most drones are more like sophisticated remote-controlled aircraft than autonomous decision-makers. Late November, though, saw some of the first tests of what &lt;a href=\"http://rt.com/usa/news/us-drone-launch-autonomous-986/\"&gt;some news outlets colourfully labelled a &ldquo;killer robot&rdquo;:&lt;/a&gt; a US Navy stealth drone piloted by artificial intelligence. Snappily named the X-47B Unmanned Combat Air System, the 19m- (62ft-) wide aircraft is designed to take split-second decisions on its own initiative &ndash; and even land itself on an aircraft carrier &ndash; while remaining under the overall control of human operators.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As much as anything, it&rsquo;s the relationship between these human operators and their subject that&rsquo;s most disturbing. Thought experiments like the trolley problem demonstrate something self-evident but extremely significant in moral thinking: how our sense of obligation is modified by distance and immediacy. We feel differently about pushing someone off a bridge with our own hands than we do about pushing a button to achieve the same result. Weapons are real-world examples of this: the arrow differs from the fist, the rifle from the arrow, the bomb from the gun. And by the time you reach autonomous systems, many of the &ldquo;actions&rdquo; setting them in motion will have taken place months or even years ago: as part of a process of programming and design whose eventual consequences may be entirely unknowable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If all this sounds rather removed from real life, it&rsquo;s worth remembering that the cutting edge of today&rsquo;s battlefield will represent little more than the state-of-the-art in domestic appliances a decade from now. Such is the speed of technological change, and the steady migration of its moral conundrums towards the mass market.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s a field in which the ghostly outlines of future controversy are already clearly visible. If a drone can oversee your child&rsquo;s journey to school today, will it be driving you both to work tomorrow &ndash; and should you insist on this, if the machine is demonstrably a safer driver than you? If robot soldiers can fight for your country without risking your sons&rsquo; and daughters&rsquo; lives, should you gratefully embrace automated warfare?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These are new questions, but answering them means grappling with ancient ethical issues about responsibility. What does it mean to hold someone responsible not only for their actions, but for the chains of consequence they set snaking through the world? And what does it mean to exercise human ingenuity responsibly, given the ever-expanding scope of our powers?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ironically, the best contemporary answer may mean giving up some of that power &ndash; and asking what it means to create machines that can take responsibility for themselves.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Tom? If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","HeadlineLong":"Moral machines raise killer questions","HeadlineShort":"Moral machines, killer questions","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Who should take responsibility for decisions made by intelligent machines like killer drones or autonomous cars?\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Who should take responsibility for decisions made by intelligent machines like killer drones or autonomous cars?","SummaryShort":"Life and death dilemmas of intelligent robots","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"1276d154-16f7-4768-8075-9851a7d22074","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121206-moral-machines-killer-questions","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121206-moral-machines-killer-questions"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121206-moral-machines-killer-questions","_id":"559a5df3157e584f77e20319"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Look up into the skies today at a passing aeroplane and the view is not that much different to the one you would have seen 60 years ago. Then and now, most airliners have two wings, a cigar-shaped fuselage and a trio of vertical and horizontal stabilizers at the tail. If it isn&rsquo;t broke, the mantra has been, why fix it, particularly when your design needs to travel through the air at several hundred miles an hour packed with people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But that conservative view could soon change. Rising fuel prices, increasingly stringent pollution limits, as well as a surge in demand for air travel, mean plane designers are going back to their drawing boards. And, now, radical new shapes and engine technologies are beginning to emerge, promising the biggest shake-up in air travel since de Haviland introduced the first commercial jet airliner in 1952.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, it would be wrong to say nothing has changed in the last few decades, says Rich Wahls, an aerodynamicist at Nasa&rsquo;s Langley Research Center in Hampton, Virginia. &nbsp;&ldquo;New model airliners don&rsquo;t come out every year like cars, but it&rsquo;s not as if they haven&rsquo;t been evolving under the skin the whole time. There&rsquo;s so much more technology in there nowadays.&rdquo; &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Earlier improvements went mostly unnoticed because they focused on building better and quieter turbine engines with higher performance and improved fuel consumption. There have also been huge strides in computer controls and fly-by-wire systems, which make a big difference to the pilot, but not to the passengers. And in recent years, the biggest development has been the use of strong, but lightweight plastics and composite materials rather than metals, reducing the weight of planes and the amount of fuel they need to burn. This has also allowed the development of &ldquo;radical&rdquo; new planes like the giant Airbus A380 and the Boeing Dreamliner.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But despite these advances, aviation engineers know there is still much to be done. Take fuel prices, for instance, which have soared in recent years. Despite modern planes being 60% to 70% more efficient than those built 60 years ago, aviation fuel expenditures now account for a quarter of an airlines operating expenses, placing them on a par with labor costs. In 2011, large US air carriers paid half again as much for fuel as what they paid in 2000. Add the fact that global airline travel is expected to grow to 3.3 billion annually by 2014 (up one third from 2009), and it&rsquo;s clear why engineers are searching for new ways to boost performance.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Shock test&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the biggest efforts to rethink the airplane is being conducted by Nasa&rsquo;s Subsonic Fixed-Wing program, a collaboration between the US aerospace agency and industrial partners including Boeing, GE, Lockheed Martin, Northrop Grumman and Pratt &amp; Whitney, as well as academic institutions such as Massachusetts Institute of Technology. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re looking to see if we can develop technologies that can get us yet another 60 to 70% improvement in fuel efficiency,&rdquo; says Wahls. In addition, the project wants to engineer new designs with a 71-decibel decrease in noise emissions and a four-fifths fall in nitrogen oxide pollutants from current standards. And, if these kinds of goals weren&rsquo;t already aggressive enough, the team wants any new technology to enter service between 2030 and 2035 - a mere blink of an eye in an industry in which commercial aircraft can have multi-decade life spans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We are trying to determine which technologies are worth pursuing; those that might get us anywhere near our goals,&rdquo; says Wahls&rsquo; boss, Ruben Del Rosario, subsonic fixed-wing program manager.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Planes under investigation by Nasa range from the extreme to the slightly more conventional. For example, Boeing&rsquo;s Sugar Volt is a design that came about as part of the manufacturers Subsonic Ultra Green Aircraft Research (Sugar) project. The design &ndash; like many new concepts - is based around the idea of maximizing the plane&rsquo;s lift. This reduces the amount of power needed to keep the plane in the air, as well as the amount of fuel it must burn.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Sugar Volt does this by using very long, narrow, flexible wings. They are so long that engineers needed to brace them with under-wing truss support struts, making the aircraft resemble the Piper Cub and other light, high-winged planes. The SugarVolt&rsquo;s wings are extended in order to increase lift, allowing shorter take-off distances and requiring less power in flight. They are so long, in fact, that the Boeing designers may have to fit them with hinges so that they could fit in existing airports and boarding gates.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Burning up&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are, however, are other ways to improve performance than just making longer wings. A team from MIT in Cambridge, Massachusetts, for example, put forward the D8 for consideration by Nasa. This &ldquo;double-bubble&rdquo; aircraft design, features a double-wide fuselage composed of two standard body cylinders melded together side-by-side, as well as low-swept wings that cut drag and weight. The idea of the wider body shape is to increase lift generated by the fuselage, rather than it being mostly dead weight slung between two wings. The extra lift and reduced drag cuts back on the quantity of fuel that the engines must burn. If the jet were built today from standard aluminum alloys it could provide a 50% reduction in fuel use, according to the MIT designers; a low-mass polymer-composite version could give 70% efficiency gains. In addition, because the D8&rsquo;s turbine engines sit on top of the fuselage in a box-shaped tail, they would cut the amount of engine noise broadcast to the ground.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The D8&rsquo;s idea for generating greater lift is taken to an extreme in another design called the N3-X hybrid wing-body airplane, which Nasa developed in-house. At first glance, the N3-X looks a lot like a so-called flying wing design, used by planes such as the US Air Force&rsquo;s B-2 stealth bomber. These comprise a single, thick triangular wing that enclose all of the plane&rsquo;s contents - cockpit, stores, engines, fuel tanks and flight surfaces. But, unlike the B-2 flying wing, the N3-X hybrid wing-body also features two thin, rather conventional wings attached to the sides of its ultra-wide fuselage.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The primary advantage of the hybrid, or blended, wing-body design is better fuel efficiency, Del Rosario says. Like a flying wing, the hybrid aircraft produces lift with its entire aerodynamic airframe, thus ridding itself of the drag associated with the cylindrical fuselage and the tail surfaces of a conventional plane. As with the D8, the more lift that can be produced overall, the less effort is needed from the engines, which in turn means less fuel must be burned. Fuel efficiency could be raised further by building the airframe from lightweight polymer composite materials instead of metals, Del Rosario says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cool running&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Engineers are aware, however, that new airframe shapes will only get them part of the way to their goals. To really make a difference, particularly to fuel consumption and engine noise, planes will also need radically new propulsion systems mounted or integrated into the airframe in novel ways. And, like car designers, aircraft manufacturers makers are beginning to explore the possibilities of electric and hybrid engines.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Boeing&rsquo;s Sugar Volt concept, for example, would use a hybrid-electric propulsion system that combines fuel-burning (turbine) engines, electric motors and electrochemical storage batteries&mdash;a propulsion concept not totally unlike that inside a Toyota Prius. The hybrid system would let the operators choose to draw engine power from the turbines or the batteries, whichever provides the most benefit for the specific segment of the flight&mdash;takeoff, landing, cruise, and so forth. &ldquo;You can envision a 737-class airliner using the combination of turbine and electric power for take off and then, depending on the situation, switching over to cruise on one or the other,&rdquo; says Marty Bradley, principal investigator for subsonic ultra-green aircraft research at Boeing Research and Technology in Bellevue, Washington.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Nasa&rsquo;s N3-X is also designed around a completely new engine concept, called turboelectric distributed propulsion. It splits the main functions of a standard turbine engine in two - generating power by burning fuel and creating thrust by blowing air rearward with a large fan.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The idea is to use two large turbine engines to drive electric generators that would produce electricity to power 15 electric motor-driven, thrust-producing fans that would be embedded across the top rear of the broad fuselage. Such a configuration could be very efficient, Del Rosario says. The array of small electric propulsion fans at the stern of N3-X enables the designers to cut drag significantly by accelerating the flow of drag-causing air moving over the upper surface of the fuselage, keeping efficiency-sapping air friction at a minimum. Like the D8, the top-mounted propulsor fans would also effectively lower noise emissions because the body would come between them and the ground below.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The airliner concept may have an Achilles&rsquo; heel, though. For such a system to reach maximum fuel-efficiency targets, the electronics, generators and motors may need to be built from superconducting (zero-resistance) materials, meaning the jet&rsquo;s electrics would have to be super-cooled by liquid hydrogen at &minus;253C (&minus;423F) or liquid nitrogen at -196C (321F) to make them work. This cryogenic technology is not yet fully practical and could take decades to prove out. Recent studies indicate, however, that substantial fuel-consumption gains could still be obtained by using existing electrical technology running at ambient temperatures, according to Del Rosario.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If that scheme sounds far out, other manufacturers are looking at developing fully electric systems for the 2050 time-frame. Aircraft engineers and designers at Eads, the parent firm of Airbus, for instance, have proposed a rather extreme concept called the Voltaire. The bulbous, 50-seat fuselage with two, long slender wings and a giant propeller on the tail, make it resemble a submarine. The concept, first put forward in 2011, would use next-generation batteries to power high-efficiency superconducting electric motors that would in turn drive the giant counter-rotating propellers mounted in a cylindrical shroud at the tail. Unlike any of the Nasa concepts, it is designed to be zero-emission.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sling shot&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, anyone thinking that the electric Voltaire airliner may fly any time soon, needs to think again, says Johannes Stuhlberger, head of the global innovation network, power and flight propulsion at Eads. &ldquo;The development of electric aircraft not only depends on the speed at which battery technology improves, but also how fast electrical equipment - the motors - get better.&rdquo; Electric motors would need efficiencies of around 95%, he adds, noting that for any new system to become a reality will require &ldquo;tremendous improvements in the power-to-mass ratio of the entire propulsion system, while still keeping it affordable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the shorter term, engineers at the Airbus group are trying to reduce fuel-consumption emissions by developing novel launch systems, similar to those found on naval aircraft carriers. In one radical concept, a low-slung carriage vehicle with an airliner mounted on its back would accelerate down the tarmac and loft the plane into the air. Such a device would substantially reduce the initial power required for a passenger plane to take off. Airbus envisages the eco-climb system moving into position automatically and assisting airliners to climb steeper and reach cruising altitude faster and from shorter runways.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, all of these developments will undoubtedly lead to changes in the passengers&rsquo; in-flight experience. Catapult take-offs will likely mean passengers will be thrust back into their seats more firmly than happens now. Ultra-wide bodied planes will likely mean fewer window per seat for the occupants and their larger seat capacities could also lead to slower passenger-deplaning procedures both at the airport and in emergency landing situations. But there will also be benefits from these new wider spaces, which could, for example, accommodate large communal social spaces for kids more commonly found on cruise ships, whilst quiet electric engines could mean a good night&rsquo;s sleep for travelers normally disturbed by the drone of turbine engines.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Are the extra carrots in the new designs worth the extra effort and costs they entail?&rdquo; asks Nasa&rsquo;s Wahls. Only time will tell. Many of these concepts are just that: concepts that are destined never to become a reality. However, like concept cars that push what is technologically possible on the road, these craft will probably inform the design of future airliners.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So, when you gaze up at the skies in twenty years time, perhaps the airplane passing by will look different from those you grew up with after all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:17:05Z","HeadlineLong":"Radical planes take shape","HeadlineShort":"Radical planes take shape","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Engineers and designers are giving commercial aircraft a makeover, in a bid to make them faster, greener and more efficient. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"Engineers and designers are giving commercial aircraft a makeover, in a bid to make them faster, greener and more efficient.","SummaryShort":"The skies will never be the same again","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:17:05Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"87176757-c7d0-4d93-ac7f-99e2dd4d8b02","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121106-radical-planes-take-shape","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:17:05Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121106-radical-planes-take-shape"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121106-radical-planes-take-shape","_id":"559a5df0157e584f77e202b1"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Readers of the Fresno Bee may have choked on their ham and eggs when they opened the paper on a Thursday morning in 1934.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Under the headline, &ldquo;Gigantic robots, controlled by wireless, to fight our battles&rdquo;, the Californian paper ran a sensational &ndash; and terrifying - story about the impending future of warfare. &nbsp;The article detailed a talk in Paris by French scientist and military engineer Professor [Felix Gaston] Gauthier in which he disclosed that &ldquo;two pacifistic-minded nations&rdquo; were &ldquo;secretly (and supposedly unknown to each other) planning to construct gigantic fighting robots.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"These mechanical soldiers,\" declared Professor Gauthier, \"will be of unexampled proportions. My informants, whose authentic statements I have never had reason to question before, have conveyed to me the startling news that each of these nations hopes someday to build robots 1,000 feet high!\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The piece went on to describe how these machines &ldquo;could crush the largest and most powerful war tank ever built by merely stamping on it&rdquo;. And, in case readers were stills struggling to imagine the size and power of these mechanical beasts, the newspaper ran a clarifying picture of two of them fighting &ndash; one raising a mace over its head ready to deliver a final blow to another automaton, which is lying on the ground amidst the wreck of buildings and tanks. The caption reads: &ldquo;In the event that the two 1,000-foot-tall enemy robots ever come face-to-face they will pound each other with their might, square iron fists, kick with their great steel legs and struggle to destroy each other while skyscrapers all around them fall to the ground&rdquo;. Quite how these machines were to be built with the technology of the day was a minor detail.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Droid fleet&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But, whilst it is easy to look back and smirk at articles like this, it should be seen in the context of the day. World War I &ndash; and the destruction it wrought - was still fresh in the minds of the &ldquo;Lost Generation&rdquo;. More than 9 million soldiers were killed, largely because technological advances such as machine guns, tanks and mustard gas. At the same time, the clouds of war were once again building over Europe. People were clinging on to any hope that the &ldquo;madness of 1914&rdquo; could be averted or &ndash; if another war was likely &ndash; that it could be fought without another terrible loss of life.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The answer, at least to some people&rsquo;s minds, was the robot. These word &ldquo;robot&rdquo; was only invented in 1920 but the concept had started to gather steam in the preceding war years.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Take an October, 1918 issue of Electrical Experimenter - published just a month before the end of WWI. The magazine was the brainchild of It showcased one of Hugo Gernsback, the &ldquo;father of science fiction&rdquo;, and it featured one of his most radical and yet most prescient ideas for the future of war: the &ldquo;Automatic Soldier.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;As science advances, and as all sorts of infernal machines are thrown into a modern war, the men in the front line trenches become less and less anxious to bear the full brunt of high explosive shells, gas attacks, liquid fire and what not,&rdquo; the article reads. &ldquo;No matter how courageous a body of soldiers, their morale is bound to deteriorate considerably under a murderous mustard gas attack, or under a modern barrage.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Gernsback&rsquo;s &nbsp;design was inspired by a Danish engineer who had obtained patents on such an invention. Pictures show a fleet of these red droids proceeding along the trenches laying waste to a fragile &ndash; and obviously German &ndash; enemy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Over the years &ndash; and with the approach of another war - terrible visions like these increased. For example, the &lt;a href=\"http://www.paleofuture.com/blog/2009/9/28/when-wars-are-fought-with-robot-soldiers-1935.html\"&gt;July&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.paleofuture.com/blog/2009/9/28/when-wars-are-fought-with-robot-soldiers-1935.html\"&gt; 28, 1935 &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.paleofuture.com/blog/2009/9/28/when-wars-are-fought-with-robot-soldiers-1935.html\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;San&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.paleofuture.com/blog/2009/9/28/when-wars-are-fought-with-robot-soldiers-1935.html\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.paleofuture.com/blog/2009/9/28/when-wars-are-fought-with-robot-soldiers-1935.html\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Antonio&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.paleofuture.com/blog/2009/9/28/when-wars-are-fought-with-robot-soldiers-1935.html\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.paleofuture.com/blog/2009/9/28/when-wars-are-fought-with-robot-soldiers-1935.html\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Light&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.paleofuture.com/blog/2009/9/28/when-wars-are-fought-with-robot-soldiers-1935.html\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;(San Antonio, TX) features illustrations from artist &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erik_Nitsche\"&gt;Erik&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erik_Nitsche\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erik_Nitsche\"&gt;Nitsche&lt;/a&gt;. These robots had machine guns for heads, robot scouts with movie camera faces, a twenty-five tube robot military band, and even a robot hospital for the repair of robot soldiers. &ldquo;When a human soldier gets a bullet in his heart, or in his liver or has himself partly blown to pieces, that is the end of that soldier,&rdquo; it reads. &ldquo;Not so with the robot. A new heart can be put in him as easily, almost, as changing a tire.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The reality, as is often the case, was less sensational. The term &ldquo;robot&rdquo; came to life in newspapers to describe the navigation units used by precision bombers that dropped their cargoes on cities across Europe, whilst the German Goliath tracked mine - a remote-controlled demolition vehicle known as the beetle tank to Allies - is regarded as one of the first &ldquo;robotic&rdquo; machines on the battlefield. The caterpillar-tracked vehicle was packed with explosives, operated by remote control and designed to drive under &ndash; and destroy &ndash; tanks. And with that, the robot was no longer an abstraction of the future.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Lost generations&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At the same time there was a shift in how the machines of war were portrayed in popular culture.&nbsp; Prior to the war, the ideas for these fantastic robotic fighting machines often came from amateur inventors and&nbsp; were depicted in lurid colour in hobby magazines and news papers. These perhaps reflected the public view of warfare but were often a world away from reality.&nbsp; But, according to Joseph Corn and Brian Horrigan in their 1984 book Yesterday&rsquo;s Tomorrows, after WWII as the world entered the Cold War era &ldquo;members of the armed services, in league with scientists and engineers in universities and the defence industry, come to monopolize the crystal ball through which tomorrow&rsquo;s weapons are glimpsed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As this view took hold and accelerated, glimpses of the world we live in today become common place, even cropping up in children&rsquo;s books. &ldquo;A robot army spearheading an attack could be far more destructive than any human force,&rdquo; reads a piece in the 1981 kid&rsquo;s&rsquo; book World of Tomorrow: Future War and Weapons. &ldquo;Its leaders would still be human though. Safely situated far behind the fighting, they would order their robot battalions into action.&rdquo; The article goes on to detail a future where &ldquo;small pilotless aircraft packed with sensors and detectors of all kinds to plot enemy positions, measure forces and eavesdrop on their communications.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Sound familiar? Today, unmanned aerial vehicles (UAVs) commonly fly missions in the ongoing wars in Afghanistan and Iraq. These drones are controlled by human beings in Nevada and for the first time in history the ability to attack an enemy in real time from the other side of the world is a reality.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The robot warriors, envisaged as a response to the horrors of World War I are now a reality. Today, the US military trains more people each year in the operation of unmanned drones than they do fighter pilots. There are currently over 7,000 unmanned aerial drones and over 12,000 robotic ground vehicles on the ground being used by the United State military. A mandate passed by the US Congress in 2002 has even dictated that a third of the US military be comprised of unmanned forces by 2015. It remains to be seen if they&rsquo;ll meet that goal.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The question, of course, is precisely where the new realities of robot warfare will take us. Just as the first atomic bomb dropped on Hiroshima in 1945 forever changed the reality and scope of war, so too might autonomous robot soldiers shift our understanding of what it means to fight and to wage war.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In some way, it seems my generation - the Millennials - are part of our own lost generation. These technologies mean we have lost any connection to war and the real world destruction that it brings. By wrapping ourselves in pixels and coaxial cable we seem to be insulating ourselves from the horrors of the battlefield. At what cost, only future generations will tell.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2012-04-04T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Robot history: The rise of the drone","HeadlineShort":"Robots go to war","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Lurid pictures from the last 100 years show the idea of military drones  and removing humans from the battlefield - has a long history.  ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Lurid pictures from the last 100 years show the idea of military drones  and removing humans from the battlefield - has a long history. ","SummaryShort":"The idea of a military drone can be traced back at least 100 years","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"bca92e81-f85e-483e-bbf9-a4337f98283d","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120403-robots-go-to-war","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120403-robots-go-to-war"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120403-robots-go-to-war","_id":"559a5ddf157e584f77e2004f"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If you want to get a sense of one of man&rsquo;s great overlooked engineering achievements look at the US Central Intelligence Agency&rsquo;s World Factbook&rsquo;s entry on roads around the world.&lt;br /&gt;&nbsp;&lt;br /&gt;It contains some interesting statistics. For example, the US contains 6.5 million km (4 million miles) of roads &ndash; the most paved country in the world. At the other end of the scale is the tiny Pacific island of &lt;a href=\"https://www.cia.gov/library/publications/the-world-factbook/geos/tv.html\"&gt;Tuvalu&lt;/a&gt; with just 8km (5 miles) &nbsp;&ndash; probably not the place to go for a driving holiday.&lt;br /&gt;&nbsp;&lt;br /&gt;But it is not always big countries that have lots of roads and vice versa. Look at countries in Africa, for example.&nbsp; Algeria, Congo and Chad &ndash; some of the largest countries on the continent &ndash; still have relatively small numbers of arteries, meaning large parts remain inaccessible and cut off from the wider world.&lt;br /&gt;&nbsp;&lt;br /&gt;In these places, delivering food, drugs or any necessity is a time-consuming, arduous process.&lt;br /&gt;&nbsp;&lt;br /&gt;But a concept called &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://matternet.net/\"&gt;the Matternet&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo; hopes to change that. The idea seems straightforward: build the &ldquo;roads&rdquo; of the future. But there&rsquo;s a big difference. These roads will be in the sky.&lt;br /&gt;&nbsp;&lt;br /&gt;The Matternet concept grew out of lengthy brainstorming sessions last summer at &lt;a href=\"http://singularityu.org/\"&gt;Singularity University&lt;/a&gt;, which is located at the NASA Research Park campus in Silicon Valley. The University was founded by Dr. Peter Diamandis, founder of the &lt;a href=\"http://www.xprize.org/\"&gt;X-Prize Foundation&lt;/a&gt;, and Dr. Ray Kurzweil, who is known for &lt;a href=\"http://www.kurzweilai.net/\"&gt;his work in artificial intelligence and transhumanism&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of those involved in those sessions was Andreas Raptopoulos, an engineer with a life-long love of flying vehicles.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"In the course, they asked us to come up with solutions to some of the globe's grand challenges,\" says Raptopoulos. \"And one of those was alleviating poverty.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Quadcopter swarm&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The more the group thought about the problem of poverty, the more they felt it was, in large part, caused by the fact that millions of people are cut off, literally, from the global economy because of a lack of delivery infrastructure.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"The concept of using roads to move stuff around is a very, very old concept,\" Raptopoulos tells me. \"The US has now more than &nbsp;miles of roads. But should Africa try to replicate that? It is expensive, and it destroys the environment.\"&lt;br /&gt;&nbsp;&lt;br /&gt;Eventually, the group considered the merits of an unconventional delivery system. Why not, they thought, use a network of unmanned aerial drones to move physical objects the way the internet carries small packets of information through various routes, and then puts all those pieces together again at the end?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;That&rsquo;s when the idea clicked for me,&rdquo; says Mint Wongviriyawong, who was also a member of the group. &ldquo;If you could use these UAVs to transport things from point-to-point, you could transport a lot of loads, autonomously, within a shorter time frame, and it could be done cheaply.&rdquo;&lt;br /&gt;&nbsp;&lt;br /&gt;The group&rsquo;s hope was to one day allow developing countries to leapfrog expensive road networks in much that &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-africa-15659983\"&gt;same way that mobile phones have allowed them to leapfrog copper wires and landlines&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&nbsp;&lt;br /&gt;They decided, in a sense, to hack the road transportation system, and they decided to do it by looking up. In particular, they focused on quadcopters, which are small, hovering aircraft with four rotors. They can take off and land vertically, are light, and run on batteries. And they can be programmed to do what you want them to. If you want to see what you can do with quadcopters, for relatively little money, check out &lt;a href=\"http://diydrones.com/\"&gt;the DIY Drones website&lt;/a&gt;. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"Our intent is to use them for delivery,\" says Caitlin Sparks, who also worked on the Matternet project last summer. \"What's really interesting is how they can be networked and routed. If you put a whole fleet of them together, it becomes a really interesting prospect.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You can imagine a very \"Jetsons-esque\" kind of future, right? With hundreds, maybe thousands of copters flying back and forth between, say, a farm in Africa and a market in a larger town. The copters would carry perhaps one or two pieces of fruit or vegetables at a time, for hours on end. It sounds inefficient compared to one big truck delivery, but Raptopoulos maintains that because the vehicles would be networked, and would function autonomously. \"You can move a lot of stuff around eventually.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And, he says, it scales. \"You would start small, with point-to-point connections,\" Raptopoulos says. \"But as the system grows, it's like nodes in a computer network. Eventually, all the small nodes could be connected into a larger scale network of UAVs, and then some day a whole logistics system.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It sounds great, but the obvious question remains: how do you even begin to build out such a system? And it is in answering that question that the original Matternet brainstorming group seems to have split along philosophical and practical lines.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Raptopoulos and Sparks have now founded a company called &lt;a href=\"http://www.matternet.us/\"&gt;Matternet&lt;/a&gt;, after the name of the original concept. They are working on creating a fleet of vehicles that can carry various weights. For now, the focus is on quadcopters because they are the cheapest and easiest to modify. But, the size of the quadcopters limits the amount that can be carried to a couple of kilograms.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"We'll start by emphasising high value payloads over volume or weight,\" says Raptopoulos. In the developing world, that would mean using the Matternet to help NGOs delivered much needed medicines, vaccines or food supplies to remote locations. In the developed world, it could mean using quadcopters in highly populated areas to carry precision electronic equipment, textiles, or pharmaceuticals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"Eventually,\" says Sparks, \"we would have a specialized fleet of different UAVs, of various sizes and different carrying capacities, that are suited to different tasks.\" They are thinking of various sizes and configurations of aircraft that could carry between five and 100kg.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;First flight&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;They are also working on how to build a network of ground stations where these vehicles can pick up and deliver goods, receive their instructions, and swap batteries when needed. They are also writing their own operating system to control the machines. They envision it as a cloud-based operating system that would run the whole network, and would be responsible for routing goods in the most efficient ways possible.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But others in the original group have a different vision.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;If the idea of the Matternet is the same as the internet, we want everyone to be able to use it and see it,&rdquo; says Arturo Pelayo. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t think it belongs to any one company.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pelayo was a member of the original brainstorming team behind the Matternet at Singularity University. He and three others, including Mint Wongviriyawong, have formed a competing company called &lt;a href=\"http://aria-logistics.com/\"&gt;aria&lt;/a&gt;, short for &ldquo;autonomous roadless intelligent array.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Aria is focused on creating the base stations for the network. Make a great platform for the network, the thinking goes, and let others create the open source applications and vehicles which they need and want.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We see ourselves as the logistics part of it,&rdquo; says Wongviriyawong. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re not focused on making things ourselves, but pulling it all together.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pelayo says he has already been contacted by many in the open source community who want to help with coding and development. Once that is done, it is over to local people to dictate how it is used.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;A village cooperative in Ghana, for example, could buy a base station,&rdquo; says Pelayo. &ldquo;The community would own that node, and figure out how best to use it.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;That&rsquo;s our passion,&rdquo; Wongviriyawong affirms. &ldquo;Let the local people figure out how best to use it, instead of us delivering a solution to them.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Currently, the subtleties of the different approaches are difficult to tease apart. For example, Sparks says that Matternet's solution is \"application agnostic\" as well. \"Both Matternet's design and business model are deeply bottom up,\" she says. \"We allow any customer to determine what they would like to do with our technology.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Perhaps it won't be until both are able to demonstrate the technology, that the differences will be clear. And we may not have to wait long.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Both companies, aria and Matternet, are pushing forward quickly, trying to get field tests up and running in the coming months.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Matternet has negotiated a contract with their first client, a global consultancy with a large presence in Turkey. They are moving forward with designs for their prototype quadcopter, and plan to run a pilot project with a UAV expert in Brazil in the coming months.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For its part, aria says it is talking with partners in the Middle East. By mid-year, the company hopes to be on the ground in various locations, conducting analysis of local customer needs. The group is also evaluating whether it the idea could work with water-based autonomous vehicles as well.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;No one approach is the best, or the only one,&rdquo; Arturo Pelayo says. &ldquo;And I think different ideas will help jump-start and inspire the entire ecosystem.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or have an idea that you would like Clark to investigate, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;This story was updated on 27 February to include&nbsp;details&nbsp;about aria.&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2012-02-22T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Matternet: Swapping roads for flying drones","HeadlineShort":"I hover dream: An end to roads","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Two start-ups want to replace road transport with swarms of tiny autonomous helicopters.
