Mercedes Dice concept allows car passengers to call up information about passing landmarks (Copyright: Daimler AG)","SynopsisMedium":"The Mercedes Dice concept allows car passengers to call up information about passing landmarks (Copyright: Daimler AG)","SynopsisShort":"The Mercedes Dice concept (Copyright: Daimler AG)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/12/07/p01207ql.jpg","Title":"mercedes-dice.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01207ql","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01207ql","_id":"559a5b2b157e584f77e18f98"}],"AssetImagePromo":[{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":116277,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/12/07/p0120797.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"(Copyright: Toyota)","SynopsisMedium":"(Copyright: Toyota)","SynopsisShort":"Toyota Fun Vii concept (Copyright: Toyota)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/12/07/p0120797.jpg","Title":"fun-vii.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p0120797","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p0120797","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p0120797","_id":"559a5b2b157e584f77e18f96"}],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Jon is the presenter of &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p002vsnb\"&gt;Science in Action on the BBC World Service&lt;/a&gt;. He trained as a mechanical engineer (with automotive and aeronautic design) before becoming a journalist. He has worked at the BBC for over a decade and has reported from areas as diverse as war zones and technology shows.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Jon Stewart","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"7559906f-3208-4907-961d-483b5b06a198","Id":"wwfuture/author/jon-stewart","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"jon-stewart"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/jon-stewart","_id":"559a5a53157e584f77e17134"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;The dangers of talking on a mobile phone or texting whilst driving are well known.&nbsp; So adding what, on first glance, look like added distractions may not seem like the best idea.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But researchers and car firms are increasingly looking to give drivers enhanced, or augmented reality (AR) that adds extra information ranging from directions to social media feeds to the view through the windscreen. And, perhaps the most unintuitive part of it is that it is designed to make the roads more secure for all of us.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The biggest way augmented reality can be useful is in supporting safe driving,&rdquo; says Professor Anind Dey, of the&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"http://www.hcii.cs.cmu.edu/\"&gt;Human-Computer Interaction (HCI) Institute&lt;/a&gt;&nbsp;at&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"http://www.cmu.edu/\"&gt;Carnegie Mellon University&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;The ability to enhance information around us that might otherwise be opaque to us is invaluable.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augmented_reality\"&gt;AR&lt;/a&gt; is usually considered to be a live view of the real world, onto which extra data &ndash; usually pulled from the internet - is layered or superimposed. It is similar to the view of the world that Arnold Schwarzenegger had in the Terminator movies. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The technology is already well established elsewhere. For example, there are numerous smartphone apps that allow users to overlay the view from their camera with everything from directions and transport options to restaurant reviews and house prices. Point your phone at something of interest, power up your app and you are away.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Companies like Google are taking it one step further. Earlier this &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/technology-17618495\"&gt;year the search giant unveiled its Google Glass project&lt;/a&gt;, a pair of minimal glasses that overlay a person&rsquo;s vision with everything from weather data, location and diary appointments. &nbsp;&nbsp;It is an idea that has been seized upon by other firms, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/technology-20462840\"&gt;such as Microsoft&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And now AR is making its way into cars. It builds on so-called &ldquo;heads up display&rdquo; (HUD) technology that is commonly used in fighter planes and began to make its way into high-end cars in the late 1990s. These HUDs are frequently used to project directions or speed onto the windscreen in the driver&rsquo;s line of sight to minimize the amount of time the driver takes their eyes off the road. But augmented reality could allow these displays to go much further than that.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Looking glass&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Car manufacturers such as BMW, Toyota, Mercedes and GM have shown off various prototypes in recent years. For example, a Mercedes concept unveiled earlier this year and known as Dice allowed drivers to conjure up information about passing places of interest by merely pointing at them while friends driving past the car show up as an icon on the windscreen, alongside their social network status.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another concept from GM, known as the enhanced vision system, uses an array of sensors and cameras mounted inside and outside the vehicle to monitor the environment around the car and the driver&rsquo;s eye and head movements. This information is then used to overlay the view from the windscreen with relevant information about driving conditions &ndash; such as the location of the building you are trying to find or dangers that you may not be aware of, such as an animal or child at the side of the road. Another mode marks the edges of the road when fog obscures the drivers view.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other concepts take AR even further. Japanese car maker Toyota has shown off a prototype of &lt;a href=\"http://reviews.cnet.com/8301-13746_7-20081402-48/toyota-demos-augmented-reality-enhanced-car-windows/\"&gt;&ldquo;enhanced car windows&rdquo;&lt;/a&gt; that would allow passengers to zoom in on places and objects of interest that they are passing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, the most extreme concept comes from designers at &lt;a href=\"http://tachilab.org/modules/projects/rpt.html\"&gt;Keio University in Japan&lt;/a&gt;, who are working on technology that will make the back seat of a car appear transparent, so that when drivers are reversing they can see everything around them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Their experimental setup uses cameras mounted outside the car &lt;a href=\"http://tachilab.org/modules/projects/transparentcockpit.html\"&gt;to project an image inside the car in the driver&rsquo;s field of view&lt;/a&gt;. Using similar technology to a heads-up display, the image is shown on a reflective screen mounted behind and between the two front seats. When a driver looks back over his shoulder, he sees the &ldquo;real&rdquo; image out of the rear view window continued, through augmented reality, right down the seats to the floor.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Innovations like this may be a long way from going into production. But other uses of AR could appear much sooner. For example, the most common use of AR is to overlay the world with directions that are overlayed on the road in front of you. Almost all concepts show some variant of this and, earlier this year, electronics firm Pioneer claimed to introduce the &lt;a href=\"http://www.engadget.com/2012/05/09/pioneer-touts-worlds-first-car-gps-with-augmented-reality/\"&gt;world&rsquo;s first GPS with AR&lt;/a&gt;, that can be installed in any vehicle.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Game view&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This kind of innovation would be helpful to any driver. But Professor Dey and his team believe it could be especially useful for &lt;a href=\"http://www.cs.cmu.edu/%7Esjunikim/download/SJKimCHI09.pdf\"&gt;older people&lt;/a&gt;, whose sense of spatial awareness may be reduced. This is especially problematic when they must look down at a navigation system, for example.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The amount of time being spent not looking out the windshield, and the number of times that occurs on a short drive, can get pretty high,&rdquo; he explains. &ldquo;Particularly when you are talking about elder drivers who may have diminished facilities for driving, you want to reduce the amount of time they are looking away.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To test this theory, he devised a simulator that projects the navigation system across the full width of the screen. The driver sees the real world, but with a map superimposed on top. As turns or streets come up, the map updates like a panning computerised window that is locked to the real world around them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although the system is a prototype, it has given Prof Dey and his team clues about how a real system should work. For example, to ensure that one set of distractions is not replaced by another, he believes that drivers should be allowed to personalize a system so that they can receive notifications from the system in ways that feel intuitive to them, such as audio tones, or even by touch, using haptic feedback through the steering wheel and seats.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;&ldquo;We should be using technologies like augmented reality to personalise the driving experience much more so that we are currently.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The trials have also flagged potential problems. For example, many of the subjects who have tried the system out in Professor Dey&rsquo;s simulator have reported that it feels like playing a computer game &ndash; this &nbsp;detachment from reality is an issue that the engineers know needs to be addressed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You don&rsquo;t want to lessen the impact of making a mistake,&rdquo; Professor Dey explains.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition, and perhaps most importantly, more work needs to be done to work out what is the optimum amount of the windscreen that can be covered with AR graphics, he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;After all, augmented reality is only useful if the driver is in touch with the reality behind it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"The future of transport. BBC presenter and trained engineer Jack Stewart sorts solar planes from magnetic trains, offering you a guide to the vehicles and transport technologies of tomorrow.","Name":"HyperDrive","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"HyperDrive","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:05:39.453487Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"5f91bd6c-5154-4e9f-9850-4ea674c4ef67","Id":"wwfuture/column/hyperdrive","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:05:39.453487Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/hyperdrive"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:05:39.453487Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"5f91bd6c-5154-4e9f-9850-4ea674c4ef67","Id":"wwfuture/column/hyperdrive","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:05:39.453487Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/hyperdrive"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/hyperdrive","_id":"559a5a68157e584f77e17453"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17490"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","HeadlineLong":"Cars turn to augmented reality ","HeadlineShort":"Cars turn to augmented reality ","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Car firms hope to turn windscreens into giant computer displays that overlay the real world with useful information, such as directions or even social media feeds. \n","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It used to be that upgrading your car would involve a trip to the garage for a new set of alloy wheels, a paint-job or perhaps a souped-up sound system.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But those days could soon be over as an increasing number of cars connect to the web. In the same way as the smartphone was changed forever by the arrival of the app, car manufacturers are now betting on these small pieces of software to drive the future of the automobile. Soon, customising your wheels could be as simple as tapping your dashboard touchscreen to download the latest must-have app.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We already see many cars that have Google onboard, and Facebook and Bloomberg updates,&rdquo; says Sven Beiker, the executive director for the center for automotive research at Stanford University in the US.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But, he adds, apps could soon go a lot further than just letting you find your favourite music or communicating with your friends. By tapping into the mass of data your car produces, combined with the huge computing resources available on the web, apps could help save you &ndash; and everyone around you &ndash; fuel, time and money.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And this is not a distant dream, he says. The groundwork for the fully connected car is being laid down now and could be with us sooner than you think.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Some people say that 2012 is the year when we will see a breakthrough in the connected car,&rdquo; says Bieker.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Car talk&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Several manufacturers including Ford, BMW, Mercedes, Audi and &lt;a href=\"http://www.marketwatch.com/story/new-cloud-based-hondalinktm-connected-car-system-helps-drivers-put-away-their-phones-while-maintaining-intuitive-access-to-their-favorite-media-2012-07-18\"&gt;most recently Honda&lt;/a&gt; already offer basic connected car systems that allow a vehicle to hook up to the web through mobile phone networks. They act as a portal to the net, but also provide practical benefits such as alerting the driver to collisions, or delays on the road ahead, and automatically finding new routes to avoid them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At the moment, these tend to be the preserve of top-end vehicles. But that too is changing. According to &lt;a href=\"http://www.abiresearch.com/press/3934\"&gt;research firm ABI Research&lt;/a&gt;, 60.1% of cars will be connected to the web by 2017, whilst in Europe and North America, the figure will be closer to 80%. Soon, cars without connectivity will be a rarity rather than the other way around.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the pioneering systems for in-car tech is Ford&rsquo;s Sync, which connects your smartphone and MP3 player to the car's dashboard. It allows drivers to make telephone calls and control the car&rsquo;s radio using their voice, amongst other things. It was developed with software giant Microsoft and was first released in 2007.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Like most of today&rsquo;s systems, the software is essentially a way of connecting the car to your smartphone. But, &lt;a href=\"http://media.ford.com/article_display.cfm?article_id=31712\"&gt;in 2010&lt;/a&gt;, Ford made the first steps towards blurring the boundaries between the mobile and the automobile when it released the Ford Sync API (application programming interface). This piece of code allowed trusted third-party developers to tap into Sync so that they could write apps that could work with &ndash; and be controlled by &ndash; the system. One of the first was the internet radio app Pandora.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It is a model that is now used by several other manufacturers. For example, German car maker BMW &lt;a href=\"http://www.bmwblog.com/2012/07/10/bmw-ready-sdk-for-third-party-apps-android-apps-coming/\"&gt;recently announced&lt;/a&gt; that it would allow vetted third-party apps developed for Google&rsquo;s Android phone software to work with its Connected Drive system (it already allowed the system to tap into some iPhone apps)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But developments like this are just the start, according to Beiker. Modern cars are incredibly sophisticated, he says, with up to 80 different computer control units that monitor everything from engine performance and braking, to direction of travel, velocity and road conditions. At the moment cars tend to keep this information to themselves. Sharing it could open up a whole new world of possibilities.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We are talking about changing the car&rsquo;s character from being introverted to being extroverted. So the car is just more talkative,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To make this a reality requires at least two important things to happen. First, car manufacturers need to make the data from their cars available, ideally in a standard format. And secondly, the data needs to be easily shared.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The first of these problems is again being tackled by manufacturers like Ford. Working with New York City-based developers &lt;a href=\"http://www.buglabs.net/\"&gt;Bug Labs&lt;/a&gt;, the car manufacturer has released a prototype system called &lt;a href=\"http://openxcplatform.com/\"&gt;OpenXC&lt;/a&gt;. Drivers install a small piece of hardware in their car which taps into the vehicles sensors and control units and spits them out in a format that can be read by compatible apps on Android.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;OpenXC allows us to investigate what developers can do when we present the car in the same way as they see a smartphone software platform,&rdquo; said Venkatesh Prasad, senior technical leader for Ford Research and Innovation, when the system debuted earlier this year. The system has since been distributed to firms and universities and has already spawned its first &ldquo;vehicle-aware&rdquo; applications.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the first was created by India's HCL Technologies and allows a driver to automatically update select contacts with his or her location. The idea is that by monitoring location and speed data from the vehicle, the app can determine if the driver will be late for a meeting and can then send an email or text message without any driver intervention.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That last bit is important. Car manufacturers are all too aware that they need to minimise distractions from these systems while driving, hence the common use of voice recognition. They maintain that many drivers already use web, communication and entertainment services in their cars, so these systems actually reduce the risk of distractions and increase safety. However, it is unlikely that it will be left to car makers to decide what is and isn&rsquo;t appropriate. Already, regulators are beginning to scrutinise these systems and earlier this year, the US National Highway Traffic Safety Administration &lt;a href=\"http://www.nhtsa.gov/About+NHTSA/Press+Releases/2012/U.S.+Department+of+Transportation+Proposes+%27Distraction%27+Guidelines+for+Automakers\"&gt;launched a report&lt;/a&gt; that recommended voluntary guidelines for car manufacturers to reduce the risk of distraction.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Always on&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Assuming that an acceptable solution can be found, the second part of the puzzle that needs to fall into place is how data is shared. At the moment, most manufacturers use proprietary computer systems, meaning that data produced by one car manufacturer is basically of little use to another. This becomes problematic for third-party app developers who would need to rewrite their code for each and every producer. But Beiker says that could soon change.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I am expecting to see more and more applications that communicate with the vehicle directly, through a standardised interface,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;I can very well imagine that over the next couple of years car companies might open up their firewall a little more, and say &lsquo;here is our app store&rsquo;.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One project attempting to solve the problem is the EU-sponsored &lt;a href=\"http://www.cvisproject.org/en/about_cooperative_systems/technologies/\"&gt;Cooperatives Vehicle Infrastructure Systems project&lt;/a&gt;, which aims to develop a &ldquo;universal communications module&rdquo; that can read data from any vehicle. The project has already published &lt;a href=\"http://www.cvisproject.org/en/news/EuropeanStandardforITSCommunicationsArchitecture.htm\"&gt;the architecture&lt;/a&gt; of the system it hopes to use.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It envisages that data would be channelled in two ways: to other cars and over mobile connections and DSRC (dedicated short-range communications) networks &ndash; a wi-fi like technology that is currently used for electronic road toll connection, for example. Car-to-car communication could be useful in safety systems, for example, allowing a car to prevent a driver pulling out of a blind junction if another vehicle is approaching or forming the basis of a peer-to-peer collision warning system to spot and advise about hazards on the road ahead.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But the intention of the mobile and DSRC network is to build an &ldquo;always-connected&rdquo; communications channel between the car and the net. This opens up the possibility of each car sharing and consuming unique cuts of information. Obvious applications include streaming entertainment apps and software that can tap into the speed and direction of travel of a car to &ldquo;crowdsource&rdquo; traffic updates. This would benefit the driver by giving them personalised driving information, but would also allow the road network as a whole to become more intelligent, allowing traffic control systems to route cars around accidents, or divert traffic around congested areas. Further down the line, these systems could tap into a cars emissions data to allow pollution credit trading in real time, or automatically reserve parking spaces by calculating when you will arrive at your location.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Hack attack&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;An always-on connection &ndash; coupled with a standardised, machine-readable data format &ndash; also raises other intriguing possibilities, such as allowing vehicles to tap into the &ldquo;cloud&rdquo; &ndash; the vast amount of computing power available on the web. This could create a host of powerful, smarter apps. For example, in 2011, Ford announced a deal with the search giant Google to use the firm&rsquo;s prediction algorithms to spot trends in large data sets.&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gamITudh6DI\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Ford's idea&lt;/a&gt;&nbsp; would send a car&rsquo;s information to Google's data centres. Over time, the algorithms would begin to predict where you are driving to every time you sit in the driver&rsquo;s seat, depending on the time of day and your usual driving habits. This would allow it to determine the most fuel-efficient journey, with the best driving conditions and the least traffic.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, as more and more cars begin to stream their telemetry, mobile networks could become overloaded. Fourth-generation cellphone systems will help, and there are already various initiatives, such as &lt;a href=\"http://www.ngconnect.org/index.htm\"&gt;the NG Connected car programme&lt;/a&gt; and the &lt;a href=\"http://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/verizon-joins-with-leading-global-auto-companies-to-establish-4g-venture-forum-for-connected-cars-157456585.html\"&gt;4G Venture Forum for Connected Cars&lt;/a&gt;, that aim to accelerate this technology. But the rise of the cloud-based app will could also spur the development of other clever connections with plenty of bandwidth.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One idea is to use wi-fi. A &lt;a href=\"http://web.mit.edu/newsoffice/2012/sharing-wifi-among-cars-0705.html\"&gt;system proposed recently&lt;/a&gt; by researchers from MIT, Georgetown University and the National University of Singapore showed how a fleet of wi-fi enabled cars could share limited wi-fi connections by shuffling data between them all, and using a select few cars to collect everyone&rsquo;s data and upload it when it finds a hotspot. The system is theoretical at the moment, but it gives an indication of the kind of technology that could begin to hit cars in the future.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another problem that will need to be solved is security. Researchers have already demonstrated that control systems in cars &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/10119492\"&gt;are vulnerable to attack&lt;/a&gt;. But once data from those critical systems &ndash; like brakes and engines &ndash; is being streamed, read and processed on the net for real, it will be even more crucial to ensure it cannot be subverted by hackers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, if the history of smartphone apps is anything to go by, this kind of obstacle will not hold the technology back. Just four years ago smartphone app stores barely existed. Today, they serve up millions of apps and billions of downloads.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Time will tell whether adding the &lt;a href=\"http://oica.net/wp-content/uploads/press-release-press-conference-20120307.pdf\"&gt;80 or so million cars that are produced every year&lt;/a&gt; into the mix will shift it up a gear or stall on the driveway.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt; page or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:16:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Connected cars open up to apps and the cloud","HeadlineShort":"Road opens for connected cars","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The automobile of the future will not just have internet access; it will depend on it, says Jon Stewart. \n","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"The car of the future will not just have internet access; it will depend on it.","SummaryShort":"Future automobiles will not just have net access... they will depend on it","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:00Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"779dc8ba-f7b4-4878-892e-65ea3a3de337","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120719-road-opens-for-connected-cars","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:00Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120719-road-opens-for-connected-cars"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120719-road-opens-for-connected-cars","_id":"559a5de7157e584f77e2019b"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Ask the owner of an electric car which feature they would like to eliminate and chances are they will say the charging cord: no one likes having to remember to plug-in their vehicle every evening.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a result, several companies including Delphi, Nissan, Qualcomm, and Rolls-Royce have either developed or tested wireless charging technology that requires drivers of electric vehicles to merely park above a device embedded in a garage floor or parking space that enables them to power up with little to no effort.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other ideas &ndash; &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120312-wireless-highway-to-charge-cars\"&gt;such as roads fitted with charging devices&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; have also been proposed, but, to date, most systems rely on a well-known technique called electromagnetic induction. It is the same technology used to charge electric toothbrushes. At its most basic, a varying electric current passes through a coil of wire in the transmitter which produces a similarly varying current in a receiver coil sitting just above. The transmitted electrical power then feeds into the battery and recharges it. In a toothbrush, the transmitter sits in the base, while the receiver sits in the brush. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But electromagnetic induction technology across an air gap &ndash; say from a recharging pad to a car - is not considered the perfect solution by everyone. Critics point out it could possibly emit stray radio waves or heat up nearby metal objects unless it is engineered just right - two issues that the wireless electric vehicle industry knows could sink the technology if the public perceive them to be dangerous. &ldquo;Perceive&rdquo; is the key word here, as the industry strongly affirms that their power transfer technology has been fully tested and shown to be completely safe. &ldquo;We crossed that threshold two years ago,&rdquo; says David Schatz, vice president of sales &amp; business development at WiTricity, a top maker of automotive recharging equipment. &ldquo;The codes and standards are being written now.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Poles apart&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But now, researchers at the University of British Columbia (UBC) in Vancouver working on how to recharge medical devices such as heart pacemakers, may have developed an alternative that avoids radio emissions altogether. They have produced a safe, high-efficiency method that employs what applied physicist and inventor Lorne Whitehead describes as &ldquo;remote magnetic gears&rdquo;. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The new non-contact power-transfer approach, Whitehead says, uses &ldquo;magneto-dynamic coupling,&rdquo; a simple magnetic field interaction between two pivoting permanent magnets that are separated by 10 to 15cm (4 to 6in) of air. &lt;a href=\"http://ow.ly/eLNxJ\"&gt;The system&lt;/a&gt; uses a transmitter magnet below and a receiver magnet up in the car. When a small electric motor turns the lower magnet, the magnetic field causes the upper one to turn, &ldquo;much as a compass follows a changing magnetic field,&rdquo; he says. The top magnet then drives a small generator that charges the car battery.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;This magnetic field between them essentially acts as a mechanical coupling - an invisible magnetic pulley/belt system, but it requires no direct contact and is almost perfectly energy efficient,&rdquo; Whitehead explains. In production the two magnets could be integrated, respectively, into the motor and the generator, making for a reliable and compact power-transfer system.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Tests show the system is more than 90% efficient compared to a cable charge,&rdquo; he says and the car does not even need to be perfectly aligned with the device. As a result, it could be built into low street curbs over which the car-borne magnet (which is installed under the bonnet) would hang, he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A demonstration system consisting of four rechargers has operated successfully on the UBC campus for about a year or so, where they wirelessly charge electric vehicles that have been retrofitted with receiver magnets. &ldquo;Since we began testing the system, the feedback from drivers has been overwhelmingly positive - all they have to do is park the car and the charging begins automatically,&rdquo; &lt;a href=\"http://science.ubc.ca/news/662\"&gt;said David Woodson&lt;/a&gt;, managing director of UBC building operations, recently.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But not everyone is convinced. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s really nothing new,&rdquo; says John M Miller, center director in the power electronics and electric machinery research group at Oak Ridge National Laboratory in Tennessee. &ldquo;Too many moving parts; it all comes down to the number of energy conversions that are involved.\" Here, the energy goes from electrical to mechanical and back to mechanical and then to electrical, the electrical engineer explained. And each step entails a loss in energy efficiency that adds up.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;WiTricity&rsquo;s Schatz was similarly skeptical, pointing out that leading automakers have already settled on designs that will be introduced &ldquo;around 2015 to 2017&rdquo;. The biggest interest is to place them in so-called plug-less hybrids, he says. In addition, the industry expects forthcoming devices to &ldquo;be approaching the size and shape of a sheet of paper and lightweight to boot,&rdquo; something that is perhaps at odds with the UBC magnetic system.&nbsp; WiTricity&rsquo;s power-transfer technology &ndash; sometimes known as wireless electricity - has no moving parts and exploits a high-efficiency coupling between transmitter and receiver&mdash;a one-step energy transfer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, the UBC team is undeterred. A patent is now in the works and Whitehead expects that the technology will be licensed to other manufacturers, most likely through a spin-off company. &nbsp;&ldquo;At this point we see no technological barriers to bringing this system to market and know no reason why it will not become the preferred mechanism for wireless charging of [electric vehicles].&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:16:50Z","HeadlineLong":"Wireless recharging: Pulling the plug on electric cars","HeadlineShort":"Pulling the plug on electric cars","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The novel technology that aims to solve battery powered cars biggest problem.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"The novel technology that aims to solve battery powered cars biggest problem.","SummaryShort":"A novel idea for wireless charging","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:50Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"555eece8-d4a2-42b4-aa8d-7ffc0a48c8f4","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121120-pulling-the-plug-on-electric-cars","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:50Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121120-pulling-the-plug-on-electric-cars"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121120-pulling-the-plug-on-electric-cars","_id":"559a5df2157e584f77e202e3"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If you want to find the smartest drivers in the world, you need to head for the home of the US car industry. Just outside Detroit, lies the town of Ann Arbor, Michigan. The drivers there are not any more intelligent than other parts of the world, despite it being a famed college town. However, their cars are.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That&rsquo;s because the roads of Ann Arbor are now home to a fleet of several thousand cars that constantly &ldquo;talk&rdquo; to one another. The scheme, known as the &lt;a href=\"http://safetypilot.umtri.umich.edu/\"&gt;Safety Pilot Model Deployment project&lt;/a&gt;, offers a potential blueprint for the future of road transport. Like many projects it aims to cut congestion and make the road network more efficient. But this vision of the future is missing one thing: crashes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The idea behind it is to improve safety by an order of magnitude,&rdquo; says Dr Peter Sweatman, director of the University of Michigan Transportation Research Institute.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although fatalities and serious injuries from road crashes have levelled off in the US, they still account for more than 32,000 deaths each year, according to the US department of Transportation. Worldwide, the World Health Organization estimates more than one million people are killed in road accidents every year.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;This is an attempt to categorically avoid crashes, by having very robust communication between vehicles,&rdquo; says Dr Sweatman.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The $22m project started in late 2011, but is on course to reach a major milestone in the coming days, when the project&rsquo;s 3,000th connected car will pull out on to the streets of Ann Arbor. Each is fitted with wireless devices to allow them to talk to each other and to other parts of the road infrastructure, such as traffic lights. The technology is known as &lsquo;Vehicle to Vehicle&rsquo; (V2V) and &lsquo;Vehicle to Infrastructure&rsquo; (V2I) communication and allows the driver to send and receive information to and from the environment around it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Real world experiment&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, the V2V allows the car to communicate with all other vehicles within a range of about 200m to 300m. They all share data such as their location, speed and direction, that is then assessed for safety risks, and if appropriate the driver is warned of any danger. For example, drivers will get an audible warning if they try to change lane with a car in their blind spot, or if the car in front of them brakes hard and the driver doesn&rsquo;t seem to notice. The car can also give warnings at blind corners and junctions that another vehicle is about to pull out. Because the communications are done by radio signals, they do not need direct line of sight, unlike a driver&rsquo;s eyes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The V2I system can also communicate with other parts of the road and traffic infrastructure. For example, there are 29 intersections in Ann Arbor that have been instrumented, so that researchers can direct the lights in order to make the traffic move more efficiently.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We&rsquo;re collecting data from these intersections, and that is being back-hauled to the city of Ann Arbor through their fibre optic network so we can collect data, and make sure the signals are all being received and processed correctly,&rdquo; Dr Sweatman explains.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To be clear, the technology fitted to the vehicles does not turn them into &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120424-robot-cars-ready-to-roll\"&gt;autonomous, self-driving cars&lt;/a&gt;, championed by the likes of Google, but they do represent an important step towards them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s hard to see how you could have automatic vehicles without them being connected,&rdquo; says Dr Sweatman. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s technically possible, but in terms of a realistic deployment autonomous vehicles will need to be connected.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120719-road-opens-for-connected-cars\"&gt;vehicle interaction systems are in development&lt;/a&gt;, and what is learnt in this huge, real world, experiment will help inform the direction they take.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;This is fundamentally aimed at safety, but clearly with a platform like this you can create many other applications,&rdquo; says Dr Sweatman. &ldquo;There are many other potential benefits in mobility, environment, emissions, energy efficiency.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, the team envisages a system that monitors the brakes of all of the cars. If the traffic management system detects that all of the cars are braking at the same spot, it could indicate a problem with the road that needs to be fixed by a maintenance crew&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Alternatively, if for some reason there was an accident, the system could be used to immediately alert the emergency services, divert other drivers to alternative routes and ensure that the roads are clear for emergency vehicles.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Currently, the majority of the cars fitted with the technology are owned by residents of Ann Arbor, who have volunteered to &lt;a href=\"http://safetypilot.umtri.umich.edu/index.php?content=participate\"&gt;join the programme&lt;/a&gt;. People take their cars in, just like a service visit at a dealership. It takes less than an hour to install the two antennas for GPS, one antenna for communications, the driver interface, and a black-box computer to link it all together.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So far, about two thirds of the drivers selected to take part in the trial are parents from Ann Arbor schools. They were deliberately chosen to ensure there are as many vehicle &ldquo;interactions&rdquo; as possible, because they tend to drive their kids around a lot, and tend to congregate in the same places. More than 60 vehicles, including buses and trucks, have also been donated by various car manufacturers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We believe that at any given time there will be 5 to 10% of these vehicles in the traffic stream,&rdquo; says Dr Sweatman.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, this number could rise dramatically if the trial &ndash; which is currently due to run until 2013 &ndash; is successful. Then, the technology could be rolled out globally. With the ability to save thousands &ndash; perhaps millions - of lives, and reduce congestion and pollution at the same time, Ann Arbor could be the blueprint for the future.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","HeadlineLong":"Building the crash-proof car","HeadlineShort":"Building the crash-proof car","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Thousands of smart vehicles are already being tested on our roads as part of an ambitious  and little known - effort to eradicate car crashes. is a science writer and broadcaster who is particularly interested in how\nhumans are transforming planet Earth and the impacts our changes are having on\nsocieties and on other species. She has visited people and places around the\nworld in a quest to understand how we are adapting to environmental change. You\ncan follow her adventures at &lt;a href=\"http://www.wanderinggaia.com/\"&gt;www.WanderingGaia.com&lt;/a&gt;\nand on Twitter at &lt;a href=\"http://twitter.com/WanderingGaia\"&gt;@WanderingGaia&lt;/a&gt;. Gaia's book, &lt;a href=\"http://www.randomhouse.co.uk/editions/adventures-in-the-anthropocene-a-journey-to-the-heart-of-the-planet-we-made/9780701187347\"&gt;Adventures In The Anthropocene&lt;/a&gt; is published in July by Chatto\n&amp; Windus.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Gaia Vince","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"57b249bc-24f8-41d1-b947-e30c33566a09","Id":"wwfuture/author/gaia","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"gaia"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/gaia","_id":"559a5a52157e584f77e1711c"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;As I said in my &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121115-the-cost-of-making-the-earth-move\"&gt;previous column&lt;/a&gt;, we are the biggest force in moving the planet&rsquo;s rocks and sediments around. Our global extractions are environmentally damaging and &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120418-no-silver-bullet\"&gt;depleting some resources&lt;/a&gt; to the extent that they are in danger of &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120308-touch-and-go-screens\"&gt;running out&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Many of those resources find their way into the goods, gadgets and machines that we find indispensable in our everyday lives. But do we really need so much stuff, or are we simply addicted to the new? There's no doubt that our consumption of resources from food to gadgets has risen dramatically over the past 60 years, and much of the world seems to be in the grip of a &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120712-beating-our-consumption-habit\"&gt;shopping epidemic&lt;/a&gt;. But is there a conscious effort driving it?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There have been many times during my travels when I've needed something repaired, from rips in my backpack, to holes in my clothes, zippers that have broken or memory cards that have lost data. From India to Ethiopia, I have had no trouble in finding someone who can sort the problem out, repair what is broken or find an ingenious way of side-stepping the issue. In rich countries, such items often would be thrown away and replaced with new ones without a second thought. But the developing world is still full of menders, make-doers and inspired users of others&rsquo; scrap. I&rsquo;ve seen a bicycle in Nairobi made from bits of car, a colander and a leather belt; aerials made from all manner of implements; and houses constructed out of old boat sails, rice sacks and plastic drinks bottles.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But then there are those items that seemingly can&rsquo;t be repaired. My camera shutter, battered by the dust and grime of travel, no longer works. I'm told I should throw away my camera, even though it works fine, apart from the shutter mechanism. Like the majority of consumer electronics, my camera has not been designed to be easily reparable. Thirty years ago, I could have found service manuals and spare parts for all camera models, as well as a thriving repair industry. But things have changed. Camera models have got far more numerous and complicated, and manufacturers no longer release repair manuals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the mobile phone handset market reached saturation in Europe and the United States about a decade ago, we have chosen not to wait for our devices to fail. Almost all new phones purchased are &ldquo;upgrades&rdquo;, replacing functioning phones simply for reasons of fashion or for technological additions that many of us rarely use, and which could otherwise easily be achieved through software upgrades to existing handsets. Indeed, most phone companies compete for business by automatically upgrading customers&rsquo; phones every year. And these smart phones use mined resources from some of the most ecologically sensitive areas of the world, as a recent &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=SE9KYQfctNk\"&gt;Friends of the Earth campaign&lt;/a&gt; points out.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Made to fail&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The idea that something that works fine should be replaced is now so ingrained in our culture that few people question it. But it is a fairly recent concept, brought about by a revolution in the advertising and manufacturing industries, which thrived on various 20th century changes, including the mass movement of large populations to cities, the development of mass production, globalisation, improved transport, international trade and public broadcast media.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The earliest example of manufacturers convincing people to frequently replace a product may be the so-called &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://documentaryheaven.com/the-lightbulb-conspiracy/\"&gt;lightbulb conspiracy&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo;, in which a group of companies is supposed to have orchestrated the Phoebus Cartel to prevent companies from selling lightbulbs with a longer than 1,000-hour lifespan (most settled on 750 hours), even though bulbs lasting more than 100,000 hours existed. The cartel said its intent was to develop international standards, but the net result was that households needed to replace their bulbs regularly, providing a far larger consumer market.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This way of selling more products by designing products that deliberately fail, cannot be repaired, or have a set lifespan imposed in some other way is known as planned obsolescence. However, it is not just a cynical plot dreamed up by manufacturers to boost profits, many politicians and economists believe it to be a societal necessity. The idea was born in the US during the 1930s depression as a way to get the economy moving again by compelling people to buy more stuff. There were plenty of factories and masses of unemployed looking for gainful work, the trouble was the people who could afford to buy things already had them. What was needed, strategists proposed, was a reason for shoppers to buy things they already had, or didn&rsquo;t know they &ldquo;needed&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;By the 1950s, planned obsolescence had become the dominant paradigm in mass production with things no longer built to last. A sophisticated advertising industry persuaded people to shop. Mechanisms flourished to make this easier, from department stores to credit. Consumerism was born. Some industries, such as fashion are predicated on planned obsolescence, with items being made to last a single season or less. Other industries are following fashion&rsquo;s high-turnover model and bringing out products that have cosmetic gimmicks or seasonal appeal but which will soon appear dated.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Computer says no&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Many people wonder whether electronic products are being designed to fail, from television sets, which have heat-sensitive condensers deliberately fitted onto the circuit board next to a heat sink connected to the transistors, to washing machines with ball-bearings fitted inaccessibly into the drums so they cannot be replaced. Specific lifespans are programmed by the manufacturers into chips in some equipment, so that printers will stop working after a preset number of pages (sending a message: &ldquo;internal error&rdquo;), coffee makers will cease functioning after a preset quota of brews, and memory cards will stop uploading after a preset number of photo uploads. The user is then forced to buy a replacement. Some computers are difficult to upgrade &ndash; new versions of software are upwardly compatible so files won&rsquo;t work on older models or, as in the new Apple MacBook Pro, the RAM is &lt;a href=\"http://ifixit.org/2763/the-new-macbook-pro-unfixable-unhackable-untenable/\"&gt;soldered to the motherboard&lt;/a&gt;, making it difficult or costly to upgrade or replace the hard drive and &lt;a href=\"http://www.pcmag.com/article2/0,2817,2405921,00.asp\"&gt;battery&lt;/a&gt;. As a result, users can find themselves buying a new computer every couple of years.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another trick is to make parts and accessories incompatible between brands or even models of the same brand. Thus, not only do consumers need to buy a different memory card or battery or charger for each device or brand of electronic equipment they buy, from phones to laptops to toothbrushes, but they must also &lt;a href=\"http://www.slate.com/articles/technology/technology/2012/09/iphone_5_dock_connector_the_one_incredibly_irksome_feature_that_will_leave_you_cursing_apple_.html\"&gt;buy a new charger or adaptor&lt;/a&gt; when they upgrade from an iPhone 4 to the iPhone 5, for example.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some consumers are starting to hit back, though, advising people on the internet how to find and remove the printer chip or over-write the memory card software. But this is technically challenging and time-consuming, and most people aren&rsquo;t even aware of the reason for their device no longer working.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Californian Kyle Wiens is the type of sophisticated electronics tinkerer that emperors of the IT industry like Microsoft and Apple dream of employing. But Kyle is a guerilla geek &ndash; he slipped through their net and crossed over to the consumer side. Instead of dreaming up intricate ways of fitting more and more components into ever-slimmer gadgets, Kyle is taking them apart.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It started a decade ago, when he was a student and his iBook stopped working, Kyle explains. &ldquo;I couldn&rsquo;t get find a service manual for it, so as my friend [fellow student Luke Soules] and I took it apart to fix it, we created our own manual.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kyle and Luke began taking apart computers and other equipment, getting around the copyright protection by creating their own service manuals from scratch, and posting them online for free. The pair called their site &lt;a href=\"http://ifixit.com/\"&gt;iFixit.com&lt;/a&gt; and over the past decade, they have expanded to hundreds more manuals, encouraging home hackers to create their own manuals for the site &ndash; &ldquo;we&rsquo;re a Wikipedia of service manuals&rdquo; &ndash; and they also sell the tools and spare parts users need to fix their own electrical devices.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;They are contributing to a cultural shift in perception, Kyle says. &ldquo;When someone has repaired their broken iPad or whatever, it&rsquo;s a life-changing experience. That person will become a different consumer. They will choose new products based on how long-lasting they are and how easy they are to take apart and repair.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Market forces&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At the moment, there is little onus on manufacturers to improve, and indeed things may be getting worse. As Apple&rsquo;s beautiful-looking and increasingly thin gadgets take an ever-larger share of the market, other manufacturers are following suit. &lt;a href=\"http://www.gsmarena.com/motorola_razr_maxx-review-800.php\"&gt;Motorola&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\"http://techcrunch.com/2012/04/15/nokia-lumia-900-review-this-ones-a-no-brainer/\"&gt;Nokia&lt;/a&gt;, both of which were known for their durable phones with easily replaced long-lasting batteries, have now both released thin phones with glued-in batteries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But some companies have been bucking the trend. HP and Dell release service manuals and make computers that are easily upgradable and repairable &ndash; &lt;a href=\"http://ifixit.com/\"&gt;iFixit.com&lt;/a&gt; recently gave HPs Z1 model a score of 10 out of 10 for repairability.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And other companies are joining the move towards a circular economy, in which economic growth is uncoupled from finite-resource-use. Instead of the linear manufacturing route: mining materials, fabricating, selling, throwing them away; a circular economy is based around making products that are more easily disassembled, so that the resources can be recovered and used to make new products, &lt;a href=\"http://www.rreuse.org/t3/\"&gt;keeping them in circulation&lt;/a&gt;. British yachtswoman Ellen MacArthur is a strong advocate of the concept and &lt;a href=\"http://www.thecirculareconomy.org/\"&gt;commissioned a report into the idea&lt;/a&gt;, which found that the benefits to Europe&rsquo;s economy alone could be $630 billion, based on cycling just 15% of materials in 48% of manufacturing and just being recycled once.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The high-end outdoors clothing company &lt;a href=\"http://www.patagonia.com/email/11/112811.html\"&gt;Patagonia&lt;/a&gt;, for example, issues a guarantee that it will repair any of its products for free over their lifetime, and employs a team of seamstresses to do just that. And in 2011, it actually ran an advertising campaign asking consumers to buy less of the stuff they don&rsquo;t need, to curb waste and environmental damage.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As mines become depleted, it could soon force the market to change its recent ways. During the course of the 20th century there was a steady fall in the price of commodities, but that price has been climbing since 2000. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re moving towards a resource-limited economy, which means manufacturers are going to have to move their business model towards remanufacturing and repairs, as the cost of rare earth metals and other resources slash profits,&rdquo; Kyle says. Perhaps, then, planned obsolescence will begin to reach its own expiration date.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Gaia?&lt;/em&gt; &lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"Choices for the future of the human planet.
