&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;tons&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;of&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;electronic&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;waste&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;, &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;or&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;e&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;-&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;waste&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;, &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;are&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;generated&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;around&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;the&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;world&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;each&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.unep.org/PDF/PressReleases/E-Waste_publication_screen_FINALVERSION-sml.pdf\"&gt;year&lt;/a&gt;. Yet, only about 10% of that is properly recycled, according to the agency. A good proportion of the rest, according the UK-based Electronic Investigation Agency (EIA), ends up being \"illegally exported and dumped in developing countries.\" These countries, &lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt;notes&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt;a&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt; 2011 &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt;report&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt;from&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.eia-international.org.php5-20.dfw1-1.websitetestlink.com/wp-content/uploads/System-Failure-FINAL.pdf\"&gt;EIA&lt;/a&gt;, simply don't have the capacity to properly recycle this kind of waste.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But that doesn't stop people from trying.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That&rsquo;s because the waste contains tiny, yet valuable, amounts of gold and copper. In fact, in a country such as Ghana, burning computer wires to reclaim the copper is, unfortunately, one of the better-paying jobs a teenager or 20-something can land.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A local businessman hands over the wires to the kids, who take the material to a place like the Agbogbloshie dump near Accra. They set it alight, wait for the plastic coatings to burn off, and then take the copper back to the businessman. Most of that copper is eventually sold to manufacturers in Western Europe, completing a grim kind of recycling.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It comes at great cost to the young kids, and to the environment. As EIA says: \"Copper wires are bundled and set alight to remove flame-resistant coatings, emitting toxic dioxins&hellip;The potential health consequences for those involved in this kind of work are dire - reproductive and developmental problems, damaged immune, nervous and blood systems, kidney damage and impaired brain development in children.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The economic reality of the situation is that most of the teams of recyclers are going to carry on what they are doing, no matter what the potential consequences. So, what is needed is a cleaner, healthier way to mine the waste for its valuable elements.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Gold mine&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And that is precisely what &lt;a href=\"http://halwatts.co.uk/\"&gt;Hal&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://halwatts.co.uk/\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://halwatts.co.uk/\"&gt;Watts&lt;/a&gt; decided to do as part of his design degree at London's Royal College of Art. He has spent the last year designing and building a device he hopes will not only offer a viable alternative to burning the wires, but may even net the youngsters who do the dirty work more money.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Watts calls his invention Esource, and it is &lt;a href=\"http://vimeo.com/44233461\"&gt;a&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://vimeo.com/44233461\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://vimeo.com/44233461\"&gt;wonder&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://vimeo.com/44233461\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://vimeo.com/44233461\"&gt;of&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://vimeo.com/44233461\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://vimeo.com/44233461\"&gt;simplicity&lt;/a&gt; that he dreamed up after witnessing first-hand the dire working conditions at the Agbogbloshie dump for himself. Watts tells me that he knew he had to create \"a system that's as cheap as possible, and that didn't require much infrastructure&rdquo;.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The first challenge was finding a cheap and reliable power source. He knew no one would want to pay the ongoing costs of running, say, a generator that powered some wire-stripping machine. He looked around, and then it hit him. \"Everyone out there has a bicycle.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The bike powers two different machines that help separate the copper from the plastic coating. First, you use a shredder that Watts specifically designed using materials available in local workshops. You feed the plastic-coated copper wires into the shredder, and the bits get ground up, smaller and smaller, until they fall through a sieve at the bottom. \"That,\" says Watts, \"guarantees that the copper and plastic are no longer stuck together.\"&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then, you use a second machine, which Watts says was inspired by gold-panning technologies. \"It has a rotating wheel with water being pumped around it,\" says Watts. As you pedal, water is pumped into the spinning wheel. Then you put the small bits of copper and plastic into it. The different weights of the particles, driven by the spiral, mean that \"the plastic washes out, and you're left with a pile of copper.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The issue, Watts admits, is throughput. The kids normally get through about 65 lbs (30kg) pounds of wire a day through burning. And, as it stands, it would be hard for them to get through that amount as quickly with Esource.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"The process is limited by the shredder,\" says Watts. \"It takes a lot of energy to shred. In testing, I got to where you could do about 11 pounds (5kg) of wire in an hour.\" Watts says that's not bad considering that &ldquo;an industrial machine that could handle 220 pounds (100kg) in an hour would cost more than $50,000.\" In contrast, Watts says he can put together his bicycle powered system for around $120.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But the low cost of the device isn't the only selling point. When you burn the wires, Watts says, you end up with bits of plastic melted onto the valuable copper. But with Esource, the copper comes out much cleaner. And clean copper, Watts notes, is worth about 20% more on the market. For exporters in Ghana, who can go through up to 110 tonnes (250,000Lbs) of copper a week, that's a lot of money. And so moving forward, Watts hopes he can make a strong economic argument to exporters to invest in the Esource system.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He also hopes to convince the young people doing the work, many of whom can make up to $15 a day, that Esource is worth it. These kids, he notes, often work in groups to burn the wires. He's hoping that instead they will pool their money, buy into an Esource set-up that they can share, and then recoup their initial expenditure through the increased value of the cleaner copper they give back to the exporters.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Watts will soon get the chance to make his case. His Esource idea recently won a grant from &lt;a href=\"http://www.watesfoundation.org.uk/\"&gt;The&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.watesfoundation.org.uk/\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.watesfoundation.org.uk/\"&gt;Wates&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.watesfoundation.org.uk/\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.watesfoundation.org.uk/\"&gt;Foundation&lt;/a&gt; to further develop his designs over the next six months. He'll be returning to Ghana in October. He has also released a freely available manual for building an Esource system, complete with ideas for different materials that might be substituted in the original design.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"I don't think there will ever be a business for me personally in making and selling the machines,\" Watts admits. \"For me, the incentive was to make it open source, and have it produced locally where it's needed. I designed it so that it could be made entirely in country.\"&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","HeadlineLong":"Esource: Cycling solution to gadget recycling","HeadlineShort":"Cycle to recycle old gadgets ","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Our desire to buy the latest must-have gadget comes at huge cost to the countries and people who deal with our electronic waste. But could pedal power offer a solution?\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"Our desire to buy the latest gadget comes at huge health cost to the people who deal with our electronic waste. But could pedal power offer a solution?","SummaryShort":"Pedal power to dispose of 'dead' devices","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"ced63359-ef5d-4f5c-9a01-783bb925b47f","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120912-cycle-to-recycle-old-gadgets","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120912-cycle-to-recycle-old-gadgets"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120912-cycle-to-recycle-old-gadgets","_id":"559a5deb157e584f77e2021b"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;No period in history compares to the &lt;a href=\"http://www.igbp.net/globalchange/greatacceleration.4.1b8ae20512db692f2a680001630.html\"&gt;Great Acceleration&lt;/a&gt; after World War II, a rapid increase in human activity driven by population expansion, globalisation, technological and communications improvement, improved farming methods and medical advances. The Great Acceleration can be seen in the &lt;a href=\"http://www.slideshare.net/owengaffney/great-acceleration\"&gt;rise in everything&lt;/a&gt; from carbon dioxide release, to water use, to number of cars, to ozone depletion, to deforestation, to GDP, to consumption.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A century ago, life expectancy in Europe and the US was less than 50 years, now it's around 80 years. I've been fortunate enough to live in an age in which I&rsquo;ve been vaccinated against killers from polio to tuberculosis to measles; I've had an inside toilet and bathroom with hot and cold running water; a refrigerator; central heating; a free education and health service; and hunger has been a pleasant and soon-relieved sensation rather than a chronic debilitating experience.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In short, like the average person living in the developed world at the end of the 20th century, my life is comfortable and far removed from that experienced by the average person a century earlier.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This rapid transformation was a filthy undertaking: pea-souper smogs shrouded cities like London killing thousands; acid rain poisoned rivers, lakes and soils, eroded buildings and monuments; refrigerant chemicals ate away at the protective ozone layer; and carbon emissions caused a change in the global climate and acidified the oceans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition, our voracious appetite for manufactured products has led to massive deforestation and created a mining boom for minerals, oil and coal that is destroying ecosystems and producing a deluge of &lt;a href=\"http://www.telegraph.co.uk/earth/environment/9253665/Great-Pacific-Garbage-Patch-has-increased-100-fold-since-the-1970s.html\"&gt;toxin and plastics waste&lt;/a&gt; that will take centuries to degrade.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lesser-developed nations will strive to follow the Great Acceleration trajectory set by industrialised nations. Peasants scraping for subsistence in the dirt of India do not toil under romanticised notions about their labours. They too would like to experience the same acceleration in life expectancy, as well as basic requirements like clean water provision, toilets, electricity, internet access, and creature comforts like cars, air conditioning, refrigerators, washing machines&hellip; the list is endless.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tightening belts&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, as global citizens sharing this planet, we cannot afford for billions more people to eat and live as &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2008/04/12/arts/television/12foot.html?_r=1\"&gt;the average American&lt;/a&gt; now does at the &lt;a href=\"http://www.famousbloggers.net/consumerism-stats-infographic.html\"&gt;expense of the environment&lt;/a&gt;. Climate change, ecosystem services degradation and limitations on natural resources threaten the lives of millions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what's the answer?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Many poor countries have argued that they must be allowed to develop their economies, with however much pollution that entails, and then clean up when they are rich. It seems like a reasonable argument, after all, that's what rich countries did. Except that rich cities still suffer from smog, much of our biodiversity has been irrevocably lost, and we're still pumping out greenhouse gases.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Others point out that there is more than enough environmental space on this planet for everyone to live sustainably, but the developed world needs to scale back its consumption and allow for a more equitable sharing of resources.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I&rsquo;ve seen both sides of the argument first hand. I spent over two years travelling around the developing world and living on the contents of a backpack. In several destinations, I washed, like the local people, with a bucket of cold water and a pouring scoop &ndash; usually river water, but in some places skin-itching seawater. In other, more touristic places, I would have access to a shower &ndash; sometimes with warm water &ndash; even though the local people rarely had such facilities. (Actually, this is part of a wider issue, highlighted in a &lt;a href=\"http://www.tourismconcern.org.uk/wet-recommendations.html\"&gt;recent report&lt;/a&gt; about how developed-world people bring their resource use on holiday with them to poor countries &ndash; something I may return to in a later column.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When I returned to London, of all the luxuries now at my disposal, I was most pleased to have drinking water on tap and a dependable hot shower. As I brought home my many boxes out of storage, I was bewildered by just how much stuff I owned. When was I going to wear so many clothes; read so many books; what was I going to do with all these accumulated items?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, not everyone living in the West is going to experience such an epiphany on excessive consumption. And, a year on, I catch myself perusing shops, wondering whether I need a new t-shirt... (I don't). But there are encouraging signs that our runaway appetite for new stuff is beginning to wane, &lt;a href=\"http://www.guardian.co.uk/environment/blog/2011/nov/01/peak-stuff-consumption-data\"&gt;in the UK&lt;/a&gt; at least, that people are eating less &lt;a href=\"http://www.theatlanticwire.com/national/2012/01/americans-are-eating-less-meat/47295/\"&gt;environmentally costly meat&lt;/a&gt;, and that Americans are copying their European counterparts in adopting &lt;a href=\"http://www.google.com/hostednews/ap/article/ALeqM5im5tlpNdAuQrAihjPR0TqYOiXZtQ?docId=46c30492445943c9917b243ec72860a7\"&gt;more fuel-efficient cars&lt;/a&gt;. Some of this is down to people having smaller disposable incomes in the recession, and some due to policy-led changes that encourage a less polluting lifestyle.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Polluter pays&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Nevertheless, these are small steps in economies based around consumerism. Citizens are encouraged to desire items from childhood onwards, and consumerism is the main economic strategy for governments trying to climb out of recession.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Meanwhile, the excessive consumption habit has spread throughout the world, and is particularly obvious in China, as I noticed on my visit earlier this year. Barely a decade ago, streams of bicyclists pedaled down alleys in Beijing. Now Chinese buy &lt;a href=\"http://articles.latimes.com/2012/jan/02/world/la-fg-china-luxury-20120103\"&gt;more Lamborghinis and Rolls-Royces&lt;/a&gt; than anybody else in the world. Shanghai&rsquo;s per capita energy use is already higher than London's, and the city has become a shoppers paradise for China&rsquo;s nouveaux rich . The four busiest Apple stores in the world are &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2011/07/25/technology/apple-sales-in-china-zoom-ahead-of-competitors.html\"&gt;in Shanghai and Beijing&lt;/a&gt;. And cities including Paris, London and New York are falling over themselves to attract this new breed of &lt;a href=\"http://knowledge.wharton.upenn.edu/article.cfm?articleid=2901\"&gt;label-obsessed consumer addicts&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One way of prompting people to be more thoughtful about their purchases &ndash; and whether such a purchase is even needed &ndash; would be to levy an environmental services fee.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href=\"http://www.guardian.co.uk/environment/2012/jul/02/polluter-pays-climate-change\"&gt;'polluter pays' principle&lt;/a&gt; already covers dirty factories in the UK and other countries, for example, who must pay to clean up their damage to soil, water or air, and to safely dispose of toxic waste. But many people think it should be extended to include carbon dioxide pollution, freshwater and energy use by manufacturers, so that the price of a product more accurately reflects its environmental cost. The charge would encourage more sustainable production and also help pay for remedial efforts from carbon capture and storage to waste water clean-up to recycling.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Socially educating people not to seek happiness through excessive consumption will be a huge and possibly doomed undertaking, however many times it is shown that &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-18187399\"&gt;it can&rsquo;t buy you happiness&lt;/a&gt;. So perhaps the answer is in trying to restore the natural balance of things.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In nature, 'resources' are cycled &ndash; for example, water taken up by plants is eaten by animals that breathe out water vapour, returning the water to the soil for the plants by way of rain. Nothing is lost. But the scale with which we humans are using resources is too rapid for natural systems to manage. Microorganisms, for example, can degrade our waste, but not at a rate that matches our production. Likewise, trees and plants can take in our carbon dioxide, but not fast enough.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ultimately, the answer lies in a synthetic version of this natural cycling, so-called &lt;a href=\"http://www.kk.org/outofcontrol/ch10-e.html\"&gt;closed-loop manufacture&lt;/a&gt;, in which products are created &lt;a href=\"http://www.oecd.org/dataoecd/15/58/43423689.pdf\"&gt;using renewable energy&lt;/a&gt;, all the materials used in the process are re-used and the final product is also fully recycled to minimise original resource-use. It means no waste, no pollution, and far greater efficiencies, as &lt;a href=\"http://www.interface.com/?r=0\"&gt;closed-loop pioneers&lt;/a&gt; are already discovering.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Guilt-free consumption could one day be possible. Whether it will make us happy is another question.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2012-07-12T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Beating our consumption habit","HeadlineShort":"Beating our consumption habit","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"We have always consumed our planet's resources but recently, our appetite has grown bigger and, with it, our impact on the planet.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Our appetite for consumerism is escalating at an uncontrollable rate. Chatfield is a British author. His\nmost recent book, Netymology, explores language and technology. He blogs at &lt;a href=\"http://tomchatfield.net/\"&gt;tomchatfield.net&lt;/a&gt; and tweets at &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/TomChatfield\"&gt;@TomChatfield&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Tom Chatfield","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"a4ca99fd-56c2-441b-98e0-a82442cab45d","Id":"wwfuture/author/tom-chatfield","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"tom-chatfield"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/tom-chatfield","_id":"559a5a55157e584f77e17187"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If you&rsquo;re looking for a headline to capture our technological times, this recent one might fit the bill: &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://spectrum.ieee.org/geek-life/hands-on/the-diy-kidtracking-drone\"&gt;The DIY kid-tracking drone&lt;/a&gt;.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To help keep a close eye on his child, American parent Paul Wallich adapted a quadcopter to follow a GPS chip in his son&rsquo;s backpack to the bus stop every morning on the way to school: an ingenious outsourcing of parental responsibility, and a formidable piece of hacking to boot. What, though, does this say about the increasing delegation not only of daily tasks to machines, but of potentially life-changing decisions themselves?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Take a life and death dilemma that could soon be science fact rather than fiction. As psychology professor Gary Marcus pointed out in a recent &lt;a href=\"http://www.newyorker.com/online/blogs/newsdesk/2012/11/google-driverless-car-morality.html\"&gt;piece for &lt;em&gt;The New Yorker&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, driverless cars are now street-legal in three American states and could soon be cruising into a garage near you. But if your driverless car were hypothetically to encounter a bus full of schoolchildren on the wrong side of the road, how should it react: should it make a decision to swerve, preventing a collision but putting your life at risk; or should it collide with the bus if there&rsquo;s a greater chance of you surviving? &ldquo;If the decision must be made in milliseconds,&rdquo; Marcus reasons, &ldquo;the computer will have to make the call.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Cars are an interesting case study because they already represent one of the most hazardous technological environments most of us enter on a regular basis &ndash; and because the belief that technology should make this environment as safe as possible has been accepted, and been saving lives, for decades. As a famous adage of product design puts it, once you&rsquo;ve built the car, your task is not simply to hope that you can prevent all accidents, but to design a better car crash &ndash; that is, to make those inevitable occasions on which things go wrong as unlikely as possible to cause fatal harm.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Minimising harm is a clear enough good. What happens, though, to ethical issues in design when the product you&rsquo;re creating is itself going to be making decisions?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Killing machine&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Marcus&rsquo; driverless car scenario riffs off the famous &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trolley_problem\"&gt;trolley problem&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo;: a thought experiment that asks subjects to decide between pushing one man off a bridge in order to save the lives of several others trapped in the path of a runaway railway trolley, or allowing that one man to survive while the others meet their doom. Most respondents are unwilling physically to push another human being to their death even if it would save multiple lives &ndash; one of the factors the test is designed to measure. A machine&rsquo;s response, though, would depend entirely upon the decision-making model encoded by its creators. One robot might refuse to act; one might save the many; one might not even recognize a choice exists. It&rsquo;s up to us to decide which program to write. But once we&rsquo;ve done it and set things in motion, it&rsquo;s up to them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Programming this into cars is one thing. Weapons are quite another &ndash; and represent perhaps the most urgent testing-ground for machine ethics today. Early this December, Iran boasted that it had &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-middle-east-20594431\"&gt;captured a US ScanEagle drone&lt;/a&gt; over its airspace; one of the most basic models of the thousands of unmanned aerial vehicles that have become the standard stuff of warfare over the last decade.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Like Paul Wallich&rsquo;s child-minding quadcopter, most drones are more like sophisticated remote-controlled aircraft than autonomous decision-makers. Late November, though, saw some of the first tests of what &lt;a href=\"http://rt.com/usa/news/us-drone-launch-autonomous-986/\"&gt;some news outlets colourfully labelled a &ldquo;killer robot&rdquo;:&lt;/a&gt; a US Navy stealth drone piloted by artificial intelligence. Snappily named the X-47B Unmanned Combat Air System, the 19m- (62ft-) wide aircraft is designed to take split-second decisions on its own initiative &ndash; and even land itself on an aircraft carrier &ndash; while remaining under the overall control of human operators.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As much as anything, it&rsquo;s the relationship between these human operators and their subject that&rsquo;s most disturbing. Thought experiments like the trolley problem demonstrate something self-evident but extremely significant in moral thinking: how our sense of obligation is modified by distance and immediacy. We feel differently about pushing someone off a bridge with our own hands than we do about pushing a button to achieve the same result. Weapons are real-world examples of this: the arrow differs from the fist, the rifle from the arrow, the bomb from the gun. And by the time you reach autonomous systems, many of the &ldquo;actions&rdquo; setting them in motion will have taken place months or even years ago: as part of a process of programming and design whose eventual consequences may be entirely unknowable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If all this sounds rather removed from real life, it&rsquo;s worth remembering that the cutting edge of today&rsquo;s battlefield will represent little more than the state-of-the-art in domestic appliances a decade from now. Such is the speed of technological change, and the steady migration of its moral conundrums towards the mass market.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s a field in which the ghostly outlines of future controversy are already clearly visible. If a drone can oversee your child&rsquo;s journey to school today, will it be driving you both to work tomorrow &ndash; and should you insist on this, if the machine is demonstrably a safer driver than you? If robot soldiers can fight for your country without risking your sons&rsquo; and daughters&rsquo; lives, should you gratefully embrace automated warfare?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These are new questions, but answering them means grappling with ancient ethical issues about responsibility. What does it mean to hold someone responsible not only for their actions, but for the chains of consequence they set snaking through the world? And what does it mean to exercise human ingenuity responsibly, given the ever-expanding scope of our powers?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ironically, the best contemporary answer may mean giving up some of that power &ndash; and asking what it means to create machines that can take responsibility for themselves.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Tom? If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"How the wired world is shaping our lives.  BBC Future unpicks how digital culture and technologies pervade everything we do.","Name":"Life:Connected","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Life:Connected","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:24:16.885549Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00534c08-7004-4218-a267-a89cd11e72fb","Id":"wwfuture/column/life-connected","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:24:16.885549Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/life-connected"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:24:16.885549Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00534c08-7004-4218-a267-a89cd11e72fb","Id":"wwfuture/column/life-connected","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:24:16.885549Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/life-connected"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/life-connected","_id":"559a5a68157e584f77e17459"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17490"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","HeadlineLong":"Moral machines raise killer questions","HeadlineShort":"Moral machines, killer questions","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Who should take responsibility for decisions made by intelligent machines like killer drones or autonomous cars?\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In the early morning of 16 June, 2012, a top secret spaceplane made a picture perfect landing at the Vandenberg Air Force Base in California. To those unfamiliar with the vehicle, it might have looked roughly similar to the US space shuttle, the manned spacecraft that shuttled astronauts into space for three decades.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But this spaceplane, called the X-37B Orbital Test Vehicle, is very different. While it looks like a plane, is launched on a rocket, has a cargo bay and uses some of the same technology as the shuttle, such as thermal shielding to protect it during reentry, it is smaller and unmanned. It is designed to stay in orbit for months on end and can automatically land back on Earth. Perhaps more crucially, the Boeing-designed plane is operated by the US Air Force and its mission is a closely held secret, prompting a slew of speculation about its true purpose.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the first X-37B was launched in 2010, amateur satellite spotters have carefully followed the robotic spacecraft&rsquo;s orbit, while those unconnected with the program have speculated that the plane could be anything from an anti-satellite weapon to so-called &ldquo;on demand reconnaissance,&rdquo; shorthand for a spy satellite that can be placed over any country in the world. Compounding the mystery was the launch of a second vehicle in 2011, which stayed in orbit for 469 days, long exceeding the Air Force&rsquo;s stated maximum requirement of 270 days for the spaceplane.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now, a third launch is slated for 11 December, according to an Air Force spokesperson, once again ramping up the rumour mill. So, what do we actually know about the plane?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tactical response&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Early reports focused on the X-37B&rsquo;s seeming resemblance, at least in size and weight, to &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_X-20_Dyna-Soar\"&gt;the X-20 Dynasoar&lt;/a&gt; (short for Dynamic Soarer), a 1950s-era hypersonic vehicle that was envisioned for a variety of military missions, including bombing and sabotaging enemy satellites. However, experts familiar with the X-37B programme emphasized that its technology is actually closer to the recently retired space shuttle (a &lt;a href=\"http://pdf.aiaa.org/preview/2011/CDReadymspace11_2426/PV2011_7315.pdf\"&gt;fact reinforced by Boeings&rsquo; proposal for a crewed version of the vehicle&lt;/a&gt; known as the X-37C). The Air Force blandly described the role of the X-37B in &lt;a href=\"http://www.af.mil/information/factsheets/factsheet.asp?id=16639\"&gt;a factsheet given to media&lt;/a&gt; as a \"reliable, reusable, unmanned space test platform&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Air Force also says the mini-shuttle has two objectives: testing &ldquo;reusable spacecraft technologies&rdquo; and conducting &ldquo;experiments which can be returned to, and examined, on Earth&rdquo;. Again, this is similar to the stated aims of the space shuttle. But many forget that earlier craft also had a secret military role. Although ostensibly a civilian program, it conducted a series of missions from 1982-1992 on behalf of the National Reconnaissance Office, carrying a series of classified spy satellites.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Similarly, most outside experts now agree that it&rsquo;s likely the robotic space plane is being used for some sort of secret reconnaissance. &ldquo;I think the guess that makes most sense is quick-response tactical imaging, meaning hours to a couple of days from request to delivery,&rdquo; says Allen Thomson, a former CIA analyst.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thomson says it is also possible that it could have a more mundane but useful task, such as &ldquo;maintaining up-to-date general purpose mapping imagery.&rdquo; However, if that is the case, Thomson says that it could be a waste of money. &ldquo;I think that the commercial satellites could and should do that cheaper and better than X-37B,&rdquo; he says. It is a view &lt;a href=\"http://www.ucsusa.org/nuclear_weapons_and_global_security/space_weapons/technical_issues/the-x-37b-space-plane.html\"&gt;backed by parts of the scientific community&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, the X-37B launches comes in the middle of a larger debate about the role of government-operated spy satellites, which have proven enormously costly but can provide some of the most advanced imagery, versus commercial satellite imagery. The US intelligence community recently slashed its budget for commercial imagery, indicating that it was going back to greater reliance on its own classified satellites.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The view that the X-37B is a reconnaissance platform is strengthened by observations from amateur satellite watchers, who track the vehicles&rsquo; orbits, and noticed that it has similar orbits to spy satellites and scientific remote sensing craft. In addition, they noticed the craft changing its orbital path several times during its test flights.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is to be expected, says Joan Johnson-Freese, professor of national security studies at the US Naval War College in Newport. &ldquo;The upcoming launch will continue just to see what the vehicle can do,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;One of the things they are testing is maneuverability. The problem with satellites in orbit is they are very predictably in certain orbits at certain times, and thus vulnerable to anti-satellite weapons (ASAT).&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Johnson-Freese says the military has long been interested in the ability of a spacecraft that has &ldquo;the ability to evade, to maneuver, to not be in a predicable place at a predicable time.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;She expects the Air Force will be &ldquo;pressing the envelope for manoeuvrability and duration&rdquo; during the X-37B&rsquo;s next flight. &ldquo;That will give them the idea of potential missions where avoidance of ASATs comes into play.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;No weapons&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although its orbits may be difficult to predict in advance, its tracks show where the X-37B has been and its likely purpose, says Brian Weeden, a technical advisor at the Secure World Foundation, a Washington-based foundation that focuses on space issues.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, he says, the X-37B flew at inclination of 42.79 degrees, which tells you how far north and south in latitude the spacecraft can see. &ldquo;The tradeoff is that something at a 90 degree polar orbit covers the whole world, but its frequency is less; it may arrive only every couple of days,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;If something is 40 or 45 degrees, it would be covering a smaller portion [of the earth], but more often. &ldquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At a 42.79 degree inclination, the X-37B would be useful for looking at a geographic region such as the Middle East, says Weeden, pouring cold water on one theory that that it was used to spy on &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-16423881\"&gt;China&rsquo;s spacelab, Tiangong-1&lt;/a&gt;. And given the current political context, he says, the Middle East &ldquo;makes sense&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weeden also suggests the X-37B&rsquo;s orbit may indicate that the military is trying out a new sensor system, such as radar imaging or &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperspectral_imaging\"&gt;hyperspectral sensors&lt;/a&gt;, which collect information across different wavelengths. He suggests this could be the case, because unlike satellites collecting light in the visible wavelength, the X-37B&rsquo;s orbits are not synchronized with the sun, a trick used to maintain a predictable angle between the sun, satellite and ground.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But, like with many of the theories surrounding the X-37B, he warns, &ldquo;it is just speculation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For its part, the Air Force itself is silent about the plane&rsquo;s use, only speaking to allay fears that it was a weapon. \"I don't know how this could be called weaponisation of space. It's just an updated version of the space shuttle type of activities in space,\" said Gary Payton, the Air Force's deputy undersecretary for space programmes, in 2010.&nbsp; \"We, the Air Force, have a suite of military missions in space and this new vehicle could potentially help us do those missions better.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Whatever its purpose; the X-37B is perhaps one of the few bright spots among the Pentagon&rsquo;s hypersonic test vehicle programs.&nbsp; The Air Force&rsquo;s X-51A Waverider, a scramjet powered hypersonic missile, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120815-hypersonic-ambitions-fall-short\"&gt;suffered a fatal mishap earlier this year on a test flight&lt;/a&gt;, and never reached hypersonic speeds.&nbsp; Separately, another hypersonic prototype, known as the Falcon Hypersonic Test Vehicle-2, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-us-canada-14497641\"&gt;suffered mishaps in both flight tests&lt;/a&gt;, plunging into the ocean.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Those other efforts, which are focused on creating missiles, are still in early testing phase, while the X-37B is clearly further along.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I think the one interesting question is whether this is just test and evaluation, or is it being used to be support real world operations,&rdquo; says Weeden. But like most questions about the plane, it is one that is currently impossible to answer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","HeadlineLong":"X-37B: Secrets of the US military spaceplane","HeadlineShort":"Secrets of US military spaceplane","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"As the US Air Force prepares for the third launch of its mysterious X-37B vehicle, BBC Future looks at what is known about the classified project. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"As the US Air Force prepares for the third launch of its mysterious X-37B vehicle, we look at what is known about the classified project. ","SummaryShort":"What is the mysterious X-37B doing?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"436db3b4-354d-4d3e-9f9a-f618199c2425","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121123-secrets-of-us-military-spaceplane","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121123-secrets-of-us-military-spaceplane"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121123-secrets-of-us-military-spaceplane","_id":"559a5df2157e584f77e202f9"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"ib2video","AssetVideoIb2":[],"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;American author, inventor, and futurist Ray Kurzweil believes it will be possible.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He argues that the exponential rise in computing power we see today will continue to a point where in 2029, machines will be as smart as people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At that point, he says, people will inevitably begin to use technology in new ways, implanting powerful devices that augment our abilities.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kurzweil calls this point in time &ldquo;the singularity&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At the &lt;a href=\"http://singularitysummit.com/\"&gt;recent Singularity Summit in San Francisco&lt;/a&gt;, he told BBC Future how this momentous change will come about and what it will mean for us and society.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","HeadlineLong":"Will men and machines merge?","HeadlineShort":"Will men and machines merge?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Will we one day be able to access all of human knowledge directly from a chip implanted into our brains?","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"video","SummaryLong":"Inventor and futurist Ray Kurzweil explains why humans and computers will become  indistinguishable in the next two decades. ","SummaryShort":" And what will it mean for the human race?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"f2d690b3-f1b2-4f9b-8cd7-8ec656fa1ddb","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121108-will-men-and-machines-merge","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121108-will-men-and-machines-merge"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121108-will-men-and-machines-merge","_id":"559a5df1157e584f77e202bd"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Think of advanced robotics, and it is easy to let your mind wander to the sentient beings depicted in Blade Runner, or the soulless, autonomous assassins in the Terminator franchise.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But, despite widespread press about armed drones hunting down terrorists and insurgents in Afghanistan and Pakistan and the increasing use of ground robots to fight roadside bombs, the truth is that most military robots are still pretty dumb. In fact, almost all unmanned systems involve humans in almost every aspect of their operations&mdash;it&rsquo;s just that instead of sitting in a cockpit or behind the wheel of a vehicle, humans are operating the systems from a joystick or computer often at a remote base far from the action.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now that is slowly beginning to change.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Next week, one of the Pentagon&rsquo;s most commonly used robots will finally make baby steps toward greater autonomy. The &lt;a href=\"http://www.irobot.com/us/robots/defense/packbot.aspx\"&gt;PackBot&lt;/a&gt;, a tracked robot used by US troops to help clear bombs in Afghanistan, will get a number of upgrades that will allows it to operate autonomously in some situations, according to Tim Trainer, a vice president for product management at iRobot, which makes the pint-sized bots.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Still, the autonomous capabilities will actually be fairly limited. In cases where the PackBot loses contact with its human operator, it will retrace its steps back to where it was when it last had communications.&nbsp; While seemingly simple, this small step toward autonomy is a critical improvement: in the past, if the robot lost communications while on its way to defuse a bomb, an explosive ordnance disposal technician would have go and retrieve it, potentially exposing the person to risk.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The upgrade includes other basic elements of autonomy, such as the ability to right itself if it falls over&mdash;a big problem in Afghanistan&rsquo;s rough terrain&mdash;and the ability to navigate between specific waypoints, using satellite navigation and overlaid imagery, without constant communications with an operator. &ldquo;Those are first steps to autonomy,&rdquo; says Trainer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The idea is to take these slow steps toward autonomous robot operations so that the military&rsquo;s confidence grows, explains Trainer. Eventually, he says, the PackBot will be able to perform more complex tasks without human intervention, such as clearing an entire building of potential threats.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While these improvements are a far cry from the notion of robotic foot soldiers, it represents the reality of where military technology is today. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think you&rsquo;ll see autonomy as the breakthrough leap,&rdquo; says Trainer. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not like we&rsquo;ll have the one autonomous solution.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Skinless Terminator&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, the Pentagon&rsquo;s progress toward fielding autonomous robotic systems has been agonizingly slow, concluded a recently released &lt;a href=\"http://www.fas.org/irp/agency/dod/dsb/autonomy.pdf\"&gt;report by the Defense Science Board&lt;/a&gt;, a panel of defence experts that advises senior Pentagon leaders. They placed a large part of the blame for a lack of autonomous robots on misperceptions about what autonomy means.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;&ldquo;Unfortunately, the word &lsquo;autonomy&rsquo; often conjures images in the press and the minds of some military leaders of computers making independent decisions and taking uncontrolled action,&rdquo; the report notes. And even though the reality is often much more prosaic&mdash;such as having a robot flip over on its own&mdash;those concerns have still served to limit the military&rsquo;s willingness to embrace autonomy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;&ldquo;It should be made clear,&rdquo; the panel says, &ldquo;that all autonomous systems are supervised by human operators at some level, and autonomous systems&rsquo; software embodies the designed limits on the actions and decisions delegated to the computer.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, statements like this do not reassure everyone. Some forward-looking robotic expert have already &lt;a href=\"http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/technology/8182003.stm\"&gt;called for more debate&lt;/a&gt; about the subject of autonomy. They point towards developments such as the SGR-A1, a gun-toting sentry robot, developed five years ago by Samsung Techwin Co for the South Korean government as a way to patrol the border between North and South Korea. The fixed robot uses pattern recognition software to spot humans and a machine gun if needed. Although the robot is designed to operate with human intervention, it is its autonomous mode that has caught ethicists&rsquo; attention as a possible precursor of future developments. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While some defense officials may downplay concerns about autonomy, Jonathan Moreno, a professor of medical ethics and health policy at the University of Pennsylvania, is urging policymakers to think now about the implications of unmanned systems, which is already having profound implications. &ldquo;From a geostrategic standpoint, it widens the scope of the battlefield - now it extends from the United States to Afghanistan and Pakistan,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;That is really new. &ldquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But for the majority of those working in the field, &ldquo;killer robots&rdquo; are not on their radar. Instead, they say, military robots are likely to be used initially for jobs that are dangerous, but not involved in actually fighting enemy forces.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Already under development, for example, is a military humanoid robot that will fight fires on ships. The innocuously named &lt;a href=\"http://www.nrl.navy.mil/media/news-releases/2012/nrl-designs-robot-for-shipboard-firefighting\"&gt;Shipboard Autonomous Firefighting Robot&lt;/a&gt;, or Saffir, actually looks suspiciously much like a skinless version of the Terminator, though its mission is much more peaceful.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Saffir, which is being developed by university researchers in cooperation with US navy scientists, is designed to fight fires on naval ships. &nbsp;But even these firefighting robots are being developed specifically to work with human counterparts, so a major focus of the work is on getting the robots to respond to human gestures and speech.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Human touch&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another robot being designed to help human soldiers is &lt;a href=\"http://www.vecnarobotics.com/solutions/bear/index.shtml\"&gt;the Bear&lt;/a&gt; (Battlefield Extraction-Assist Robot), a humanoid robot with tracked legs. The Bear, built by Vecna Technologies, was initially funded by the US Army and designed to scoop up injured soldiers from the battlefield, transporting them to a safe area where they could receive medical assistance. The idea was that in the middle of a firefight it would be better to send in a robot to rescue a person, rather than another soldier who might get injured.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But Vecna Technologies's chief technology officer, Daniel Theobald says that the company now is focusing on other missions for its robotics work. &ldquo;We quickly realized if we could build a robot that could rescue a wounded soldier, it would also be capable of a lot of other high value activities,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the missions the company has focused in on is logistics, or the idea of having robots move things from one place to another, or loading and unloading supplies. While not as glamorous perhaps as battlefield rescues, this sort of work is still critical to keeping human soldiers out of danger.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;A lot of time when soldiers get wounded, it&rsquo;s because they are doing some other activity, and couldn&rsquo;t keep their hands on their weapons,&rdquo; says Theobald.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Moving supplies is also a mission envisaged for &lt;a href=\"http://www.bostondynamics.com/robot_bigdog.html\"&gt;Boston Dynamic&rsquo;s Big Dog&lt;/a&gt;, which has captured public attention for its eerily mammal-like movements.&nbsp; But like other robotic efforts, Big Dog&rsquo;s road to autonomy is slow - its designers would like it to be able to sense objects or barriers on its own, for example, but it would ultimately be supervised by a human.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, in Afghanistan, for example, just moving things around has become a dangerous job, because it exposes troops on the ground to roadside bombs, which continues to be the leading cause of battlefield deaths there. Put robots in the place of the humans, and, at least in theory, lives would be saved.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Theobald says that when it comes to the battlefield, the first autonomous robots are likely going to be essentially deliverymen.&nbsp; &ldquo;Let the soldiers do the fighting,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;Moving things around, that&rsquo;s something that the robots can do.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","HeadlineLong":"Next generation military robots have minds of their own","HeadlineShort":"Battle bots think for themselves","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"A number of robots in development for the military are being given increasing amounts of autonomy. The question is now how they will be used. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"A number of robots in development for the military are being given increasing amounts of autonomy. The question is now how they will be used. ","SummaryShort":"The next generation of military machines ","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"fc42b531-3228-4180-8bed-4fe91536c19e","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120928-battle-bots-think-for-themselves","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120928-battle-bots-think-for-themselves"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120928-battle-bots-think-for-themselves","_id":"559a5dec157e584f77e2023d"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Gort in the original The Day The Earth Stood Still; Bender from Futurama; the huge Talos in Jason &amp; The Argonauts; Robby in Forbidden Planet; the Gunslinger in Westworld; Huey, Dewey &amp; Louie in Silent Running; ED-209 from Robocop, and the (admittedly obscure) Yo-Yo in 70s TV series Holmes &amp; Yo-Yo. They&rsquo;re just a few of my favourite film robots, and that&rsquo;s without bringing in androids, replicants, cyborgs or semi-sentient computers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The list rushed through my head this week when I &lt;a href=\"http://phx.corporate-ir.net/phoenix.zhtml?c=176060&amp;p=irol-newsArticle&amp;ID=1674133&amp;highlight=\"&gt;read the news&lt;/a&gt; that the online bookstore-turned Aladdin&rsquo;s cave Amazon had bought a robotics firm called Kiva. Could the firm do for robotics what its Kindle has done electronic books? Would we all soon have sophisticated personal robots at our side... or at least to tuck us up in bed with an electronic story? In short, the answer seems to be a disappointing no. &lt;a href=\"http://www.kivasystems.com/\"&gt;Kiva builds &ldquo;order fulfilment solutions&rdquo;&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; hi-tech, and no doubt sophisticated, orange wagons for retrieving orders in warehouses. But orange wagons nonetheless.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Like a lot of people, I find it hard to separate robots that exist from their all pervasive fantasy-world counterparts. In part, that&rsquo;s because ever since George Melies&rsquo; The Clown and the Automaton in 1897 we have been fed a slow drip diet of walking, talking mechanoids. And there&rsquo;s always something new along to reinforce it: like the recent Simpsons episode where Mr Burns replaces all the power plant workers with Asimo-style robots, all voiced by Brent Spiner, best known as Star Trek&rsquo;s android Data.&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The real ones have walked or, far more often, not walked among us for over half a century.&nbsp; They are in every part of the world in ever increasing numbers &ndash; an estimated 150,000 new ones took up their places last year (according to the &lt;a href=\"http://www.ifr.org/\"&gt;International Federation of Robotics&lt;/a&gt;).&nbsp;&nbsp; And yet the mental profile many of us have of them is so inaccurate we would struggle to identify a robot if it appeared in front of us.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Centurion army&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Unimate, the first industrial robot began working at a General Motors factory in New Jersey in 1961, lifting and stacking chunks of hot metal straight from a die-stamping machine.&nbsp; Its inventor, who had the wonderfully futuristic name of George Devol, only died last year. Although Unimate was an impressive bit of design, robots have taken huge strides since then &ndash; not literally, that&rsquo;s The Iron Giant you&rsquo;re probably thinking of &ndash; with major advances in every aspect of how they look and see, react and interact, compute and cogitate.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And nowhere is that more apparent than in Japan &ndash; one of the few societies that has embraced robots &ldquo;in the wild&rdquo;. There, robots are considered one of the country&rsquo;s best hopes of meeting the challenge of its ageing population - there are now more than five million people in the country born before the word &ldquo;robot&rdquo; was even introduced by Karel Capek in his 1920 play R.U.R.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a result, Japanese researchers recently unveiled a whole mechanised army developed specifically to tend to the elderly, from the Hospi-Rimo robot to keep them linked to doctors and others, to a 24 fingered hair-washing robot, to a robot bed that morphs into an electric wheelchair.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If that sounds like a seniors version of Transformers, then there&rsquo;s definite shades of Iron Man to another of their advances - a robotic suit designed to give the infirm extra strength and mobility.&nbsp; I have visions of Tokyo at the mercy of a new kind of armoured centurion, 100+ year olds stomping around in all all-powerful exo-skeletons like wrinkly Mechagodzillas.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course the reality of most robots is that they are designed to do all sorts of repetitive, difficult and dangerous tasks - including, perhaps most famously, the kind of bomb disposal shown in 2009&rsquo;s The Hurt Locker. The trailer-towing machine in the film&rsquo;s opening scenes is an HD-1 made by Tennessee company Remotec, and it&rsquo;s almost unique in cinema history in being a reasonably faithful representation of a robot.&nbsp; Particularly because the HD-1 isn&rsquo;t made to look slickly hi-tech: it requires delicate handling and struggles with the conditions.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It features in one of the tensest sequences in a recent best picture Oscar-winner....and yet I doubt anyone would nominate HD-1 &ndash; even if they knew its name &ndash; as a favourite fictional robot.&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The dominance of the real by the imaginary is so great I used to wonder what would happen if you went out on the street with a mime artist dressed in something that looks vaguely futuristic and with their face painted silver, and got them to slowly and &ldquo;robotically&rdquo; respond to commands.&nbsp;&nbsp; I suspected quite a few passers-by would be likely to believe this was the latest robot out of the labs in Japan.&nbsp;&nbsp; More likely than if you showed them a bed that transformed into a wheelchair or something with 24 fingers that gave you a cut and blow dry.&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I say used to wonder, because earlier this week I was talking to one of the UK&rsquo;s best known roboticists, Noel Sharkey,&nbsp;Professor of Artificial Intelligence and Robotics at Sheffield University, and I told him about my robo-mime idea.&nbsp; &ldquo;Already been done&rdquo;, he told me.&nbsp; &ldquo;I know a couple of &lsquo;people robots&rsquo; who act it out all the time&rdquo;.&nbsp;&nbsp; And is anyone fooled?&nbsp;&nbsp; &ldquo;Really fooled&rdquo;, he laughed, &ldquo;one of them was pretending to be R2D2 in Dublin and she got kicked down the stairs... by boys who thought she was a machine.&nbsp;&nbsp; So she was pretty convincing even when she screamed&rdquo;. &nbsp;&nbsp;Maybe that&rsquo;s a job for a real robot instead.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Quentin?
