X-37B Orbital Test Vehicle is a US Air Force unmanned, space plane whose purpose is shrouded by secrecy. (Copyright: US Air Force)","SynopsisMedium":"The X-37B Orbital Test Vehicle is a US Air Force unmanned, space plane whose purpose is shrouded by secrecy. (Copyright: US Air Force)","SynopsisShort":"Classified data","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/11/lj/p011lj64.jpg","Title":"front-view.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p011lj64","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p011lj64","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p011lj64","_id":"559a5b28157e584f77e18f31"}],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Sharon is a 2012/13 fellow at the Woodrow Wilson\nInternational Center for Scholars in Washington DC, where she is working on a\nhistory of the Pentagon's Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency. Her\nwriting on military science and technology has appeared in Nature, Discover,\nSlate, Wired, the Washington Post Magazine, and the Financial Times, among\nother publications. She is the co-author of A Nuclear Family Vacation: Travels\nin the World of Atomic Weaponry (Bloomsbury, 2008) and the author of Imaginary Weapons:\nA Journey Through the Pentagon's Scientific Underworld (Nations Books, 2006).&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Sharon Weinberger","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"ae67fb1f-feba-497e-9404-7677e5d775e1","Id":"wwfuture/author/sharon-weinberger","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"sharon-weinberger"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/sharon-weinberger","_id":"559a5a54157e584f77e17172"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In the early morning of 16 June, 2012, a top secret spaceplane made a picture perfect landing at the Vandenberg Air Force Base in California. To those unfamiliar with the vehicle, it might have looked roughly similar to the US space shuttle, the manned spacecraft that shuttled astronauts into space for three decades.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But this spaceplane, called the X-37B Orbital Test Vehicle, is very different. While it looks like a plane, is launched on a rocket, has a cargo bay and uses some of the same technology as the shuttle, such as thermal shielding to protect it during reentry, it is smaller and unmanned. It is designed to stay in orbit for months on end and can automatically land back on Earth. Perhaps more crucially, the Boeing-designed plane is operated by the US Air Force and its mission is a closely held secret, prompting a slew of speculation about its true purpose.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the first X-37B was launched in 2010, amateur satellite spotters have carefully followed the robotic spacecraft&rsquo;s orbit, while those unconnected with the program have speculated that the plane could be anything from an anti-satellite weapon to so-called &ldquo;on demand reconnaissance,&rdquo; shorthand for a spy satellite that can be placed over any country in the world. Compounding the mystery was the launch of a second vehicle in 2011, which stayed in orbit for 469 days, long exceeding the Air Force&rsquo;s stated maximum requirement of 270 days for the spaceplane.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now, a third launch is slated for 11 December, according to an Air Force spokesperson, once again ramping up the rumour mill. So, what do we actually know about the plane?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tactical response&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Early reports focused on the X-37B&rsquo;s seeming resemblance, at least in size and weight, to &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_X-20_Dyna-Soar\"&gt;the X-20 Dynasoar&lt;/a&gt; (short for Dynamic Soarer), a 1950s-era hypersonic vehicle that was envisioned for a variety of military missions, including bombing and sabotaging enemy satellites. However, experts familiar with the X-37B programme emphasized that its technology is actually closer to the recently retired space shuttle (a &lt;a href=\"http://pdf.aiaa.org/preview/2011/CDReadymspace11_2426/PV2011_7315.pdf\"&gt;fact reinforced by Boeings&rsquo; proposal for a crewed version of the vehicle&lt;/a&gt; known as the X-37C). The Air Force blandly described the role of the X-37B in &lt;a href=\"http://www.af.mil/information/factsheets/factsheet.asp?id=16639\"&gt;a factsheet given to media&lt;/a&gt; as a \"reliable, reusable, unmanned space test platform&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Air Force also says the mini-shuttle has two objectives: testing &ldquo;reusable spacecraft technologies&rdquo; and conducting &ldquo;experiments which can be returned to, and examined, on Earth&rdquo;. Again, this is similar to the stated aims of the space shuttle. But many forget that earlier craft also had a secret military role. Although ostensibly a civilian program, it conducted a series of missions from 1982-1992 on behalf of the National Reconnaissance Office, carrying a series of classified spy satellites.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Similarly, most outside experts now agree that it&rsquo;s likely the robotic space plane is being used for some sort of secret reconnaissance. &ldquo;I think the guess that makes most sense is quick-response tactical imaging, meaning hours to a couple of days from request to delivery,&rdquo; says Allen Thomson, a former CIA analyst.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thomson says it is also possible that it could have a more mundane but useful task, such as &ldquo;maintaining up-to-date general purpose mapping imagery.&rdquo; However, if that is the case, Thomson says that it could be a waste of money. &ldquo;I think that the commercial satellites could and should do that cheaper and better than X-37B,&rdquo; he says. It is a view &lt;a href=\"http://www.ucsusa.org/nuclear_weapons_and_global_security/space_weapons/technical_issues/the-x-37b-space-plane.html\"&gt;backed by parts of the scientific community&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, the X-37B launches comes in the middle of a larger debate about the role of government-operated spy satellites, which have proven enormously costly but can provide some of the most advanced imagery, versus commercial satellite imagery. The US intelligence community recently slashed its budget for commercial imagery, indicating that it was going back to greater reliance on its own classified satellites.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The view that the X-37B is a reconnaissance platform is strengthened by observations from amateur satellite watchers, who track the vehicles&rsquo; orbits, and noticed that it has similar orbits to spy satellites and scientific remote sensing craft. In addition, they noticed the craft changing its orbital path several times during its test flights.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is to be expected, says Joan Johnson-Freese, professor of national security studies at the US Naval War College in Newport. &ldquo;The upcoming launch will continue just to see what the vehicle can do,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;One of the things they are testing is maneuverability. The problem with satellites in orbit is they are very predictably in certain orbits at certain times, and thus vulnerable to anti-satellite weapons (ASAT).&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Johnson-Freese says the military has long been interested in the ability of a spacecraft that has &ldquo;the ability to evade, to maneuver, to not be in a predicable place at a predicable time.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;She expects the Air Force will be &ldquo;pressing the envelope for manoeuvrability and duration&rdquo; during the X-37B&rsquo;s next flight. &ldquo;That will give them the idea of potential missions where avoidance of ASATs comes into play.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;No weapons&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although its orbits may be difficult to predict in advance, its tracks show where the X-37B has been and its likely purpose, says Brian Weeden, a technical advisor at the Secure World Foundation, a Washington-based foundation that focuses on space issues.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, he says, the X-37B flew at inclination of 42.79 degrees, which tells you how far north and south in latitude the spacecraft can see. &ldquo;The tradeoff is that something at a 90 degree polar orbit covers the whole world, but its frequency is less; it may arrive only every couple of days,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;If something is 40 or 45 degrees, it would be covering a smaller portion [of the earth], but more often. &ldquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At a 42.79 degree inclination, the X-37B would be useful for looking at a geographic region such as the Middle East, says Weeden, pouring cold water on one theory that that it was used to spy on &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-16423881\"&gt;China&rsquo;s spacelab, Tiangong-1&lt;/a&gt;. And given the current political context, he says, the Middle East &ldquo;makes sense&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weeden also suggests the X-37B&rsquo;s orbit may indicate that the military is trying out a new sensor system, such as radar imaging or &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperspectral_imaging\"&gt;hyperspectral sensors&lt;/a&gt;, which collect information across different wavelengths. He suggests this could be the case, because unlike satellites collecting light in the visible wavelength, the X-37B&rsquo;s orbits are not synchronized with the sun, a trick used to maintain a predictable angle between the sun, satellite and ground.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But, like with many of the theories surrounding the X-37B, he warns, &ldquo;it is just speculation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For its part, the Air Force itself is silent about the plane&rsquo;s use, only speaking to allay fears that it was a weapon. \"I don't know how this could be called weaponisation of space. It's just an updated version of the space shuttle type of activities in space,\" said Gary Payton, the Air Force's deputy undersecretary for space programmes, in 2010.&nbsp; \"We, the Air Force, have a suite of military missions in space and this new vehicle could potentially help us do those missions better.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Whatever its purpose; the X-37B is perhaps one of the few bright spots among the Pentagon&rsquo;s hypersonic test vehicle programs.&nbsp; The Air Force&rsquo;s X-51A Waverider, a scramjet powered hypersonic missile, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120815-hypersonic-ambitions-fall-short\"&gt;suffered a fatal mishap earlier this year on a test flight&lt;/a&gt;, and never reached hypersonic speeds.&nbsp; Separately, another hypersonic prototype, known as the Falcon Hypersonic Test Vehicle-2, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-us-canada-14497641\"&gt;suffered mishaps in both flight tests&lt;/a&gt;, plunging into the ocean.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Those other efforts, which are focused on creating missiles, are still in early testing phase, while the X-37B is clearly further along.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I think the one interesting question is whether this is just test and evaluation, or is it being used to be support real world operations,&rdquo; says Weeden. But like most questions about the plane, it is one that is currently impossible to answer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"Where science, security and secrets collide. Defence writer Sharon Weinberger goes behind the closed doors of the labs and projects working on the future of national security.","Name":"Code Red","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Code Red","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:26:38.302606Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"bc48772e-9411-4996-bc62-45072bc8e05b","Id":"wwfuture/column/code-red","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:26:38.302606Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/code-red"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:26:38.302606Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"bc48772e-9411-4996-bc62-45072bc8e05b","Id":"wwfuture/column/code-red","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:26:38.302606Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/code-red"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/code-red","_id":"559a5a68157e584f77e1744f"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17490"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","HeadlineLong":"X-37B: Secrets of the US military spaceplane","HeadlineShort":"Secrets of US military spaceplane","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"As the US Air Force prepares for the third launch of its mysterious X-37B vehicle, BBC Future looks at what is known about the classified project. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Look up into the skies today at a passing aeroplane and the view is not that much different to the one you would have seen 60 years ago. Then and now, most airliners have two wings, a cigar-shaped fuselage and a trio of vertical and horizontal stabilizers at the tail. If it isn&rsquo;t broke, the mantra has been, why fix it, particularly when your design needs to travel through the air at several hundred miles an hour packed with people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But that conservative view could soon change. Rising fuel prices, increasingly stringent pollution limits, as well as a surge in demand for air travel, mean plane designers are going back to their drawing boards. And, now, radical new shapes and engine technologies are beginning to emerge, promising the biggest shake-up in air travel since de Haviland introduced the first commercial jet airliner in 1952.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, it would be wrong to say nothing has changed in the last few decades, says Rich Wahls, an aerodynamicist at Nasa&rsquo;s Langley Research Center in Hampton, Virginia. &nbsp;&ldquo;New model airliners don&rsquo;t come out every year like cars, but it&rsquo;s not as if they haven&rsquo;t been evolving under the skin the whole time. There&rsquo;s so much more technology in there nowadays.&rdquo; &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Earlier improvements went mostly unnoticed because they focused on building better and quieter turbine engines with higher performance and improved fuel consumption. There have also been huge strides in computer controls and fly-by-wire systems, which make a big difference to the pilot, but not to the passengers. And in recent years, the biggest development has been the use of strong, but lightweight plastics and composite materials rather than metals, reducing the weight of planes and the amount of fuel they need to burn. This has also allowed the development of &ldquo;radical&rdquo; new planes like the giant Airbus A380 and the Boeing Dreamliner.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But despite these advances, aviation engineers know there is still much to be done. Take fuel prices, for instance, which have soared in recent years. Despite modern planes being 60% to 70% more efficient than those built 60 years ago, aviation fuel expenditures now account for a quarter of an airlines operating expenses, placing them on a par with labor costs. In 2011, large US air carriers paid half again as much for fuel as what they paid in 2000. Add the fact that global airline travel is expected to grow to 3.3 billion annually by 2014 (up one third from 2009), and it&rsquo;s clear why engineers are searching for new ways to boost performance.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Shock test&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the biggest efforts to rethink the airplane is being conducted by Nasa&rsquo;s Subsonic Fixed-Wing program, a collaboration between the US aerospace agency and industrial partners including Boeing, GE, Lockheed Martin, Northrop Grumman and Pratt &amp; Whitney, as well as academic institutions such as Massachusetts Institute of Technology. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re looking to see if we can develop technologies that can get us yet another 60 to 70% improvement in fuel efficiency,&rdquo; says Wahls. In addition, the project wants to engineer new designs with a 71-decibel decrease in noise emissions and a four-fifths fall in nitrogen oxide pollutants from current standards. And, if these kinds of goals weren&rsquo;t already aggressive enough, the team wants any new technology to enter service between 2030 and 2035 - a mere blink of an eye in an industry in which commercial aircraft can have multi-decade life spans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We are trying to determine which technologies are worth pursuing; those that might get us anywhere near our goals,&rdquo; says Wahls&rsquo; boss, Ruben Del Rosario, subsonic fixed-wing program manager.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Planes under investigation by Nasa range from the extreme to the slightly more conventional. For example, Boeing&rsquo;s Sugar Volt is a design that came about as part of the manufacturers Subsonic Ultra Green Aircraft Research (Sugar) project. The design &ndash; like many new concepts - is based around the idea of maximizing the plane&rsquo;s lift. This reduces the amount of power needed to keep the plane in the air, as well as the amount of fuel it must burn.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Sugar Volt does this by using very long, narrow, flexible wings. They are so long that engineers needed to brace them with under-wing truss support struts, making the aircraft resemble the Piper Cub and other light, high-winged planes. The SugarVolt&rsquo;s wings are extended in order to increase lift, allowing shorter take-off distances and requiring less power in flight. They are so long, in fact, that the Boeing designers may have to fit them with hinges so that they could fit in existing airports and boarding gates.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Burning up&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are, however, are other ways to improve performance than just making longer wings. A team from MIT in Cambridge, Massachusetts, for example, put forward the D8 for consideration by Nasa. This &ldquo;double-bubble&rdquo; aircraft design, features a double-wide fuselage composed of two standard body cylinders melded together side-by-side, as well as low-swept wings that cut drag and weight. The idea of the wider body shape is to increase lift generated by the fuselage, rather than it being mostly dead weight slung between two wings. The extra lift and reduced drag cuts back on the quantity of fuel that the engines must burn. If the jet were built today from standard aluminum alloys it could provide a 50% reduction in fuel use, according to the MIT designers; a low-mass polymer-composite version could give 70% efficiency gains. In addition, because the D8&rsquo;s turbine engines sit on top of the fuselage in a box-shaped tail, they would cut the amount of engine noise broadcast to the ground.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The D8&rsquo;s idea for generating greater lift is taken to an extreme in another design called the N3-X hybrid wing-body airplane, which Nasa developed in-house. At first glance, the N3-X looks a lot like a so-called flying wing design, used by planes such as the US Air Force&rsquo;s B-2 stealth bomber. These comprise a single, thick triangular wing that enclose all of the plane&rsquo;s contents - cockpit, stores, engines, fuel tanks and flight surfaces. But, unlike the B-2 flying wing, the N3-X hybrid wing-body also features two thin, rather conventional wings attached to the sides of its ultra-wide fuselage.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The primary advantage of the hybrid, or blended, wing-body design is better fuel efficiency, Del Rosario says. Like a flying wing, the hybrid aircraft produces lift with its entire aerodynamic airframe, thus ridding itself of the drag associated with the cylindrical fuselage and the tail surfaces of a conventional plane. As with the D8, the more lift that can be produced overall, the less effort is needed from the engines, which in turn means less fuel must be burned. Fuel efficiency could be raised further by building the airframe from lightweight polymer composite materials instead of metals, Del Rosario says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cool running&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Engineers are aware, however, that new airframe shapes will only get them part of the way to their goals. To really make a difference, particularly to fuel consumption and engine noise, planes will also need radically new propulsion systems mounted or integrated into the airframe in novel ways. And, like car designers, aircraft manufacturers makers are beginning to explore the possibilities of electric and hybrid engines.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Boeing&rsquo;s Sugar Volt concept, for example, would use a hybrid-electric propulsion system that combines fuel-burning (turbine) engines, electric motors and electrochemical storage batteries&mdash;a propulsion concept not totally unlike that inside a Toyota Prius. The hybrid system would let the operators choose to draw engine power from the turbines or the batteries, whichever provides the most benefit for the specific segment of the flight&mdash;takeoff, landing, cruise, and so forth. &ldquo;You can envision a 737-class airliner using the combination of turbine and electric power for take off and then, depending on the situation, switching over to cruise on one or the other,&rdquo; says Marty Bradley, principal investigator for subsonic ultra-green aircraft research at Boeing Research and Technology in Bellevue, Washington.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Nasa&rsquo;s N3-X is also designed around a completely new engine concept, called turboelectric distributed propulsion. It splits the main functions of a standard turbine engine in two - generating power by burning fuel and creating thrust by blowing air rearward with a large fan.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The idea is to use two large turbine engines to drive electric generators that would produce electricity to power 15 electric motor-driven, thrust-producing fans that would be embedded across the top rear of the broad fuselage. Such a configuration could be very efficient, Del Rosario says. The array of small electric propulsion fans at the stern of N3-X enables the designers to cut drag significantly by accelerating the flow of drag-causing air moving over the upper surface of the fuselage, keeping efficiency-sapping air friction at a minimum. Like the D8, the top-mounted propulsor fans would also effectively lower noise emissions because the body would come between them and the ground below.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The airliner concept may have an Achilles&rsquo; heel, though. For such a system to reach maximum fuel-efficiency targets, the electronics, generators and motors may need to be built from superconducting (zero-resistance) materials, meaning the jet&rsquo;s electrics would have to be super-cooled by liquid hydrogen at &minus;253C (&minus;423F) or liquid nitrogen at -196C (321F) to make them work. This cryogenic technology is not yet fully practical and could take decades to prove out. Recent studies indicate, however, that substantial fuel-consumption gains could still be obtained by using existing electrical technology running at ambient temperatures, according to Del Rosario.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If that scheme sounds far out, other manufacturers are looking at developing fully electric systems for the 2050 time-frame. Aircraft engineers and designers at Eads, the parent firm of Airbus, for instance, have proposed a rather extreme concept called the Voltaire. The bulbous, 50-seat fuselage with two, long slender wings and a giant propeller on the tail, make it resemble a submarine. The concept, first put forward in 2011, would use next-generation batteries to power high-efficiency superconducting electric motors that would in turn drive the giant counter-rotating propellers mounted in a cylindrical shroud at the tail. Unlike any of the Nasa concepts, it is designed to be zero-emission.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sling shot&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, anyone thinking that the electric Voltaire airliner may fly any time soon, needs to think again, says Johannes Stuhlberger, head of the global innovation network, power and flight propulsion at Eads. &ldquo;The development of electric aircraft not only depends on the speed at which battery technology improves, but also how fast electrical equipment - the motors - get better.&rdquo; Electric motors would need efficiencies of around 95%, he adds, noting that for any new system to become a reality will require &ldquo;tremendous improvements in the power-to-mass ratio of the entire propulsion system, while still keeping it affordable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the shorter term, engineers at the Airbus group are trying to reduce fuel-consumption emissions by developing novel launch systems, similar to those found on naval aircraft carriers. In one radical concept, a low-slung carriage vehicle with an airliner mounted on its back would accelerate down the tarmac and loft the plane into the air. Such a device would substantially reduce the initial power required for a passenger plane to take off. Airbus envisages the eco-climb system moving into position automatically and assisting airliners to climb steeper and reach cruising altitude faster and from shorter runways.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, all of these developments will undoubtedly lead to changes in the passengers&rsquo; in-flight experience. Catapult take-offs will likely mean passengers will be thrust back into their seats more firmly than happens now. Ultra-wide bodied planes will likely mean fewer window per seat for the occupants and their larger seat capacities could also lead to slower passenger-deplaning procedures both at the airport and in emergency landing situations. But there will also be benefits from these new wider spaces, which could, for example, accommodate large communal social spaces for kids more commonly found on cruise ships, whilst quiet electric engines could mean a good night&rsquo;s sleep for travelers normally disturbed by the drone of turbine engines.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Are the extra carrots in the new designs worth the extra effort and costs they entail?&rdquo; asks Nasa&rsquo;s Wahls. Only time will tell. Many of these concepts are just that: concepts that are destined never to become a reality. However, like concept cars that push what is technologically possible on the road, these craft will probably inform the design of future airliners.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So, when you gaze up at the skies in twenty years time, perhaps the airplane passing by will look different from those you grew up with after all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:17:05Z","HeadlineLong":"Radical planes take shape","HeadlineShort":"Radical planes take shape","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Engineers and designers are giving commercial aircraft a makeover, in a bid to make them faster, greener and more efficient. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"Engineers and designers are giving commercial aircraft a makeover, in a bid to make them faster, greener and more efficient.","SummaryShort":"The skies will never be the same again","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:17:05Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"87176757-c7d0-4d93-ac7f-99e2dd4d8b02","Id":"wwfuture/story/20121106-radical-planes-take-shape","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:17:05Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20121106-radical-planes-take-shape"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20121106-radical-planes-take-shape","_id":"559a5df0157e584f77e202b1"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;When Chuck Yeager broke the sound barrier in 1947, it ushered in a new era of high-speed air travel. Now, engineers are trying to make the next leap to craft that can fly more than five times the speed of sound. But it is proving difficult. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The most recent test flight &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/news/technology-19277620\"&gt;ended in failure&lt;/a&gt; on Tuesday when a faulty control fin caused the US Air Force &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_X-51\"&gt;X-51 Waverider &lt;/a&gt;jet to lose control and crash into the Pacific Ocean.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The missile-like vehicle -&nbsp; powered by a supersonic combustion engine known as a scramjet - was dropped from a B-52 bomber off the coast of southern California&nbsp; It was supposed to be propelled by a solid-rocket booster, then ignite its scramjet engine to reach speeds of up to Mach 6. In the end, the test flight lasted just 31 seconds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&nbsp; was the last of three planned tests of the X-51, designed to demonstrate the feasibility of a hypersonic missile. It now joins a long list of failed hypersonic flights that show just how difficult it is to reach these so-called hypersonic speeds, usually defined as Mach 5 or above.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Rush to failure&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The appeal of hypersonics is simple: imagine an aircraft that can travel from New York to London in under an hour, or a missile that can reach anywhere in the world in less than two hours. But military experts have long cautioned that missiles, rather than reusable aircraft, are likely to be what will be developed first.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;An air-to-air missile or an even an air-to-ground missile is the most likely near-term application for hypersonics, says Werner Dahm, director of Security and Defense Systems Initiative at Arizona State University, and a former US Air Force chief scientist. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s technologically much more achievable in the near to midterm,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Engineers have taken a variety of approaches to hypersonic aircraft over the years: the X-51 is called a WaveRider because it literally rides its own shock waves, and is powered by a scramjet, a variation of the traditional ramjet engine, where the exhaust from fuel combustion is compressed as it goes through the engine. But the Pentagon has also looked at a number of rocket-boosted gliders, and more complicated reusable aircraft powered by combination turbine and ramjet engines, among other designs.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But even building a test vehicle has proved difficult: the first X-51 flight test was cut short due to a flight anomaly, and the second test failed after the vehicle didn&rsquo;t separate from its rocket, as planned. Yesterday&rsquo;s failure is likely to raise even more questions about the future of hypersonic efforts. &ldquo;Hypersonics test and evaluation is extremely unforgiving of miscalculation and error,&rdquo; says Richard Hallion, a former senior advisor to the Air Force, and a leading expert on hypersonics.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, hypersonics has a mixed history, littered with the bodies of cancelled test vehicles, particularly those that have proved too ambitious. Hallion says many hypersonic research programs have suffered from a &ldquo;rush to failure&rdquo;, where flight vehicles have been flown too early, and then failed not because of an inherent problem in the vehicle, but because of a simple engineering mistake.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most memorable, perhaps, was the 1980s-era &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rockwell_X-30\"&gt;National Aero-Space Plane&lt;/a&gt;, which was touted by President Ronald Reagan as a new Orient Express that could travel from Washington, DC to Tokyo in two hours, reaching speeds of up to 25 times the speed of sound. But the test aircraft, dubbed the X-30, proved too costly and vastly too complicated for the technology at the time. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;More recently, the Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency (Darpa), the research and development arm of the Pentagon, tried to revive the idea of a reusable hypersonic aircraft through a program called &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA_Falcon_Project#Blackswift\"&gt;Blackswift&lt;/a&gt;, though it was soon canceled, after Congress questioned the ability to engineer such an aircraft, given previous failures.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But that has not put them off the idea of hypersonic flight. The Pentagon also recently started work on the High Speed Strike Weapon, a hypersonic missile that will be launched from an aircraft. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s the next step,&rdquo; says Mark Lewis, a former Air Force chief scientist, who was involved in the X-51 effort. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s looking at making hypersonics more operational and practical.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another programme is also underway: Darpa, which has also funded the X-51, held an open meeting this week for those interested in bidding on a new hypersonic program, which according to the agency announcement, is expected to lead to a hypersonic &ldquo;X-plane&rdquo; in 2016.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Monumental challenge&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The programme, however, is based on the design of the &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA_Falcon_Project\"&gt;Falcon Hypersonic Test Vehicle-2&lt;/a&gt;, or HTV-2, an unmanned glider that can travel at speeds of up to Mach 20, but suffered two previous catastrophic failures. The HTV-2 was designed to be launched off a rocket, and then glide, unpowered. But in its first flight, the vehicle began to spin like a football eventually crashing; the second flight also failed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;HTV-2 was once considered as a candidate for a mission known as &ldquo;Prompt Global Strike,&rdquo; a weapon that could travel anywhere in the world within an hour.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For critics of the HTV-2, Darpa&rsquo;s decision to continue with a vehicle that already failed twice in flight for apparent design failures is a repeat of mistakes made in previous hypersonic programmes. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a bad design. It flew twice, it was lost twice,&rdquo; says Hallion. &ldquo;No matter how much lipstick you put on this pig, it&rsquo;s still a pig.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some advocates of hypersonics, however, believe that the real problem with the government and military programmes is that they try to do much, and then simply cut off support when things go wrong. &ldquo;You cannot achieve this until you commit yourself to doing it,&rdquo; says Preston Carter, who previously managed hypersonics programs at Nasa, Darpa, and the Lawrence Livermore Laboratory in California,&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Carter is now in the private sector trying to find funding for a commercial aircraft designed to travel at speeds of around Mach 5, which would make the trip from New York to London in about half the time that it took the Concorde.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hypersonics is challenging, Carter acknowledges, but it&rsquo;s not impossible. He points to previous aircraft, like the SR-71 Blackbird, a supersonic spy plane, and the civilian Concorde that, though not hypersonic, pushed the envelope of what was technically possible. Those aircraft also had seemingly monumental engineering challenges at the start, but they eventually succeeded, Carter says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The same approach needs to be taken to hypersonic aircraft. &ldquo;The reason why they were able to do it because they started,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;The reason we won&rsquo;t do it is because we haven&rsquo;t started.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This story was updated at 2010GMT on 15 August to state that a faulty control fin caused the failure of the X-51. &lt;em&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2012-08-15T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"X-51 Waverider: Hypersonic jet ambitions fall short","HeadlineShort":"Hypersonic ambitions fall short","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The dreams of being able to fly from New York to London in under an hour are once again put on hold, as the latest effort to fly at over five times the speed of sound ends in failure. \n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"The latest attempt to fly at over five times the speed of sound joins the long list of efforts that have ended in failure. ","SummaryShort":"The latest effort to fly at over five times the speed of sound ends in failure","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:50Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"a531b67a-72d1-41d2-87e8-ed9cdc7ad2fd","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120815-hypersonic-ambitions-fall-short","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:50Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120815-hypersonic-ambitions-fall-short"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120815-hypersonic-ambitions-fall-short","_id":"559a5de9157e584f77e201d9"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In late September, a secretive experimental vehicle roared into the clear blue skies above a military base deep within the Arctic Circle in Norway. As the sleek, rocket approached its target altitude of 350km (218 miles), it began to arc back to earth, gradually accelerating to so-called hypersonic speeds of up to Mach 8 &ndash; about 9,800km/h (6,100 mph).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The test was the fifth of nine planned launches for the Hifire vehicle, which its &lt;a href=\"http://www.dsto.defence.gov.au/news/6947/\"&gt;backers claim &lt;/a&gt;&nbsp;&ldquo;could be a major step forward in the quest for hypersonic flight&rdquo;, generally regarded as Mach 5 and above. At these speeds, headline writers like to say, we could soon be zipping from London to New York in just one hour.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It is a promise that is often repeated about Hifire and other vehicles, such as the experimental US Air Force X-51A WaveRider, that had its latest (unsuccessful) test in August. Yet, delve back in history, you find similar promises.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the pages of popular books, magazines and newspaper comics, the hyperfast world of airline travel was predicted to be just over the horizon. There was seemingly no limit to humanity&rsquo;s capability to zip about the globe with increasing speed. In his 1965 book &lt;a href=\"http://books.google.com/books/about/Supersonic_Transport.html?id=PoDSYgEACAAJ\"&gt;Supersonic&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://books.google.com/books/about/Supersonic_Transport.html?id=PoDSYgEACAAJ\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://books.google.com/books/about/Supersonic_Transport.html?id=PoDSYgEACAAJ\"&gt;Transport&lt;/a&gt;, Irwin Stambler charts the progression of time it took to cross the Atlantic in history: from 350 hours on wooden ships to 120 hours on steam ships to 60 hours in dirigibles to 12 hours prop planes to 6 hours in planes of the very near future. The graph continues and projects forward to when the one hour barrier would be passed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It was written at a time when air travel was emerging as a reasonably affordable option for many middle class people and there was reason to be optimistic that not only would prices continue to fall, but jets would continue to get faster.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Needle point nose&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This period of optimism started on 14 October 1947, when Air Force pilot Charles &ldquo;Chuck&rdquo; Yeager dropped from the bomb bay of a B-29 bomber in the experimental X-1, a rocket-powered airplane that was the first to break the sound barrier.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the following years, the prospect of supersonic &ndash; and faster - air travel was always just around the corner. On 22 January 1953, for example, the Gleaner, a paper based in Kingston, Jamaica, carried a story from the Associated Press with the headline &ldquo;London to N.Y in one hour seen&rdquo;. The story quoted a talk given by the then chief executive of British Overseas Airways to the Aircraft Recognition Society. \"In the next 50 years our grandchildren will probably be looking at supersonic commercial aircraft carrying up to 500 passengers at fares cheaper than third class travel today,&rdquo; he was quoted as saying.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The first vehicles to begin to test these claims &ndash; as with today&rsquo;s hypersonic craft &ndash; were built and operated by the military. This was in part out of necessity and precedent. But, as Stambler notes in his book, building a military plane and a commercial &ldquo;supersonic transport&rdquo; for passengers are two completely different challenges. Providing an acceptable experience for paying customers (taking into account high temperatures, appropriate cabin pressurization, and so on) is one of the obvious and yet daunting challenges of non-military high-speed aircraft.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But that didn&rsquo;t stop people trying. In Europe, the UK and French governments subsidised designs that would eventually become Concorde, while in Russia, plans were revealed form the &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-144\"&gt;Tupolev Tu-144&lt;/a&gt;, nicknamed Concordski for its similarity to the European craft.&nbsp;&nbsp; In the US, various firms hawked competing designs. In a &lt;a href=\"http://books.google.com.au/books?id=99sDAAAAMBAJ&amp;pg=PA86&amp;dq=true&amp;redir_esc=y#v=onepage&amp;q=true&amp;f=true\"&gt;1960&rsquo;s Popular Mechanics article&lt;/a&gt; titled &ldquo;Here&rsquo;s a peek at tomorrow&rsquo;s huge planes&rdquo;, the writer describes two different designs from North American Aviation and Lockheed. The North American Aviation was designed primarily for military use, but Lockheed focused on the mass market.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Lockheed officials, arguing that there is now no technical , operational or economic reason why a supersonic transport could not be developed in the US, suggest that its shape could be needed-pointed fore and aft, and that it have a swept back stabilizer near the front end of the fuselage,&rdquo; it reads. &ldquo;Passengers would sit forward of the delta wing.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The steel plane would cost $160m to develop, it says, but the firm believed it could sell up to 200 of them at $9,240,000 each.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;By the early 1960s, Concorde was given the go ahead. However, its high cost meant that the French Aerospatiale and the British Aircraft Corporation had to combine forces. Their final design was revealed in March 1969, when the sleek-nosed aircraft climbed into the sky for the first time.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Speeding bird&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the run up to this 27 minute maiden flight, supersonic transport was a recurring theme in popular culture. For example, the July 11, 1965 edition of the Sunday comic strip Our New Age, written by &lt;a href=\"http://blogs.smithsonianmag.com/paleofuture/2012/01/sunday-funnies-blast-off-into-the-space-age/\"&gt;Athelstan&lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://blogs.smithsonianmag.com/paleofuture/2012/01/sunday-funnies-blast-off-into-the-space-age/\"&gt; &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://blogs.smithsonianmag.com/paleofuture/2012/01/sunday-funnies-blast-off-into-the-space-age/\"&gt;Spilhaus&lt;/a&gt;, promised readers that supersonic commercial aircraft would become a reality by 1972. The comic strip, taking its cues from Concorde, explained that these high-speed vehicles will look &ldquo;just like paper darts - just one triangular wing and a vertical tail - but built of titanium alloys to withstand the heat.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The strip explained that while the 200 passenger supersonic planes of tomorrow would travel supersonically above (45,000 feet), they would have to take off and descend slowly in order to not disturb people on the ground, and even break windows in areas around the airport.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The last frame of the strip paints a glorious picture for the wide-eyed airline passenger of tomorrow: &ldquo;cruising at altitudes over 70,000 feet, you may see aurora, a dark blue sky and the curvature of the earth - while going three times the speed of sound.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Alas, it wasn&rsquo;t meant to be; at least not for long. &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concorde\"&gt;Concorde&lt;/a&gt; began commercial flights in 1976, becoming one of only two supersonic passenger planes to ever fly. But in perhaps the most blatant affront to the theory of exponential technological growth (at least that the market will sustain), ceased flights 27 years later following a deadly crash and ongoing concerns about safety and cost. No commercial supersonic transport has so far replaced it, despite &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120405-supersonic-jets-ready-to-take-off\"&gt;various designs being put forward&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although Concorde set many records, it did not come close to the mythical one hour crossing between New York and London. Its fastest crossing occurred on 7 February 1996 when Captain Leslie Scott flew from the US to the UK in two hours 2 hours 52 minutes and 59 seconds. The Press Association reported that the Concorde &ldquo;breezed into the record books - thanks to 175mph tail winds across the Atlantic.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The aircraft averaged more than 1,250 miles an hour all the way from take-off to landing - travelling a mile every three seconds,&rdquo; the report read. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other craft have admittedly come closer. In 1974 two American Air Force officers flew their SR-71A &ldquo;Blackbird&rdquo; from New York to London in one hour 54 minutes 56.4 seconds. But, it seems that despite the promises of yesteryear, a one hour flight still remains firmly in the future.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","HeadlineLong":"The quest to fly from New York to London in one hour","HeadlineShort":"'New York to London in one hour'","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"A trans-Atlantic journey of just sixty minutes has been promised since the dawn of supersonic flight, but is it now closer to reality?   <script type="text/javascript">
   (function() {
    'use strict';

    var promoManager = {
        url: '',
        segments: ['a', 'b'],
        promoLoaded: false,
                makeUrl: function (variant, theme, win) {
            var loc = win? win.location : window.location,
                proto = loc.protocol,
                host = loc.host,
                url = proto + '//' + ((proto.match(/s:/i) &amp;&amp; !host.match(/^www\.(int|test)\./i))? 'ssl.' : 'www.'),
                themes = ['light', 'dark'];

            if ( host.match(/^(?:www|ssl)\.(int|test|stage|live)\.bbc\./i) ) {
                url += RegExp.$1 + '.';
            }
            else if ( host.match(/^pal\.sandbox\./i) ) {
                url += 'test.';
            }
 
                        theme = themes[ +(theme === themes[0]) ];
           
           return url + 'bbc.co.uk/navpromo/' + variant + '/' + theme;
        },
                validSegment: function (segment) {
            var validSegments = this.segments;
            
            for (var i = 0, len = validSegments.length; i &lt; len; i++) {
                if (validSegments[i] === segment) {
                    return segment;
                }
            }
            
            return validSegments[0];
        },
                init: function(node) {
            var disabledByCookie = (document.cookie.indexOf('ckns_orb_nopromo=1') &gt; -1),
                orbFullWidth     = (document.getElementById('orb-aside').offsetWidth &gt;= 1008),
                that = this;
            
            if (window.promomanagerOverride) {
                for (var p in promomanagerOverride) {
                    that[p] = promomanagerOverride[p];
                }
            }
                
            if ( window.orb.fig('uk') &amp;&amp; orbFullWidth &amp;&amp; !disabledByCookie ) {
                require(['orb/async/_footerpromo', 'istats-1'], function(promo, istats) {
                                        var mandolinEndDate = new Date().getTime() + (7 * 60 * 60 * 24) * 1000,
                        mandolin = new bbc.Mandolin('footer-promo', that.segments, {rate: 0.2, end: mandolinEndDate}),
                        segmentToRequest = that.validSegment(mandolin.getSegment());

                    that.url = (window.promomanagerOverride || that).makeUrl(segmentToRequest, 'light');

                    if (that.url) { 
                        promo.load(that.url, node, {
                                                          onSuccess: function(e) {
                                istats.addLabels({ 'campaignID': e.campaignID });
                                if (segmentToRequest === mandolin.getSegment()) {
                                    istats.addLabels({ 'promo_id_segment': e.campaignID + ':' + mandolin.getSegment() });
                                }
                                istats.track('internal', {region: node, linkLocation : 'orb-footer-promo'});

                                istats.log('display', 'orb-footer-promo-displayed', {campaignID : e.campaignID, testVariant: segmentToRequest});
                                node.className = node.className + ' orb-footer-promo-loaded';
                                promoManager.promoLoaded = true;
                                promoManager.event('promo-loaded').fire(e);
                             },
                             onError: function() {
                                istats.log('error', 'orb-footer-promo-failed');
                                document.cookie = 'ckns_orb_nopromo=1; expires=' + new Date(new Date().getTime() + 1000 * 60 * 10).toGMTString() + ';';
                             }
                        });   
                    }
                });
            }
        }
    };
    
        
    define('orb/promomanager', ['orb/lib/_event'], function (event) {
        event.mixin(promoManager);
        return promoManager;
    });
    
    require(['orb/promomanager'], function (promoManager) {
        promoManager.init(document.getElementById('orb-footer-promo'));
    })
})();
  </script>
